[1] Chapter 1
[2] The Fugitive Jeanne
[3] "In short, I just don't like her," the waitress with the bird's nest hairdo murmured. But her voice wasn't really low, and the only customer in "The Happy Coffee Shop" could hear her easily. He was curious. Was she talking about another waitress or another ordinary customer like himself?
[4] "Why should I like her? Do as you please."
[5] "I think she's quite nice," the other waitress, a short, round-faced woman, said, but it sounded less certain than before.
[6] "She's like that when her pride is hurt. Once she gets her spirits up again, her mouth becomes venomous. It should be the other way around. I know many people like that, and you should never trust them."
[7] "The other way around? What do you mean?" the round-faced waitress asked.
[8] Every Thursday evening at 7:30, Hercule Poirot dined at "The Happy Coffee Shop." He smiled now. He knew what a bird's nest was. Her powers of observation were always so keen.
[9] "It's forgivable to say cutting things when you're in trouble. I admit, I've done it myself. I want everyone around me to be happy when I'm happy. That's just how it should be. Some people treat others the worst when they're in the best of circumstances, and you should be careful with people like that."
[10] Hercule Poirot thought, yes, that was wisdom [1].
[11] The door of the coffee shop suddenly burst open, hitting the wall. A woman stood at the door, wearing a light brown coat and a deep brown hat, with golden hair cascading down her shoulders. Poirot couldn't see her face clearly. She turned her head and seemed to be looking back, as if someone was chasing her.
[12] Within a few seconds, the cold air of the night had chased the warmth from the small coffee shop, starting from the entrance. Normally, Poirot would be angry about this, but today, he was interested in the newly arrived guest. Her arrival was dramatic, and she didn't care about the impression she left.
[13] Poirot covered the cup with his palm, trying to keep the coffee warm. The small, crooked coffee shop was located in St. Gregory's Alley in London, not a very respectable place. However, Poirot believed that the coffee's taste was unmatched anywhere else in the world. Normally, he wouldn't drink coffee before or after a meal, but every Thursday evening at 7:30, he would come to "The Joyful Coffee Shop" and make an exception.
[14] However, Poirot didn't like many of the coffee shop's other habits. The cutlery, napkins, and water glasses were originally neatly placed on the table, but after Poirot arrived, he always felt they were not in the right positions. The waitress seemed to think that any spot on the table was fine, but Poirot had different ideas. Whenever he came, he would insist on how the cutlery, napkins, and water glasses should be arranged.
[15] "Excuse me, miss. Could you please close the door when you come in?" the waitress with the nest of hens scolded the woman at the door wearing a brown coat. The woman held the door frame with one hand and still faced the street. "Even if you don't want to come in, we don't want to catch a cold."
[16] The woman entered and closed the door. She didn't apologize for leaving the door open for so long. She was panting, and everyone in the café could hear it, but she seemed not to notice that there were other people in the café. Poirot greeted her with a soft "Good evening," but she only glanced at him and said nothing. Her eyes were wide open, filled with an unusual fear, as if a strong hand could tightly grip a stranger.
[17] Poirot's calm demeanor was now disturbed.
[18] The woman hurriedly walked to the window and stared outside. Poirot guessed that she saw nothing. Through the glass, from the bright room to the dark outside, one could only see the reflections of people and objects in the room. But she stood there, staring outside, as if she had to see something on the street.
[19] "Oh, it's you," the curly-haired waitress said impatiently. "What's going on? What happened?"
[20] The woman in brown coat turned around and choked, "No, I... I mean, can I sit in the corner?"
[21] "Besides that gentleman's seat, you can sit anywhere, everything is already prepared," Poirot said. "Your dinner is being prepared with care," he added. Poirot was pleased to hear this. The food at "The Happy Coffee Shop" was almost as good as its coffee. It was hard to believe, considering the kitchen staff were all British. It was truly remarkable!
[22] The woman with the tousled hair turned to the distraught woman and said, "Jane, are you really okay? You look like you've seen a ghost."
[23] "I'm fine. Thank you. Please give me a cup of hot strong tea, as usual," Jane said hurriedly, heading towards a distant table. She didn't even look at Poirot as she passed by. Poirot subtly moved his chair to observe her. She had something on her mind, it was clear she just didn't want to talk to the staff at the coffee shop.
[24] She sat down in a chair far from the door, still wearing her hat and coat. She turned around and looked behind her again. Poirot took the opportunity to closely examine her face. She was around forty years old, with large blue eyes that were wide open and unblinking. Poirot thought her eyes seemed to see a horrifying scene, as the woman with the tousled hair had said, "seen a ghost." However, Poirot observed nothing of the sort around them, only the tables and chairs of the coffee shop, the wooden hat rack in the corner, and the crooked shelves with various-sized teapots.
[25] These crooked shelves were enough to make one shudder. Poirot couldn't understand why they couldn't replace them with a proper one. He also couldn't comprehend why they couldn't place the forks parallel to the table's edge when they put them on the table. Poirot had long accepted these conveniences and inconveniences, as did most people.
[26] Jenny, huddled in her seat, stared intently at the door of the coffee shop, as if waiting for someone to burst in at any moment. Perhaps it was because of the cold, she shivered.
[27] No. Poirot suddenly changed his mind. It wasn't because of the cold. The coffee shop had warmed up again. Jenny was fully focused on the door, but she sat far away from it, facing away from it. There was only one conclusion to this.
[28] Poirot picked up his coffee cup, got up from his seat, and walked towards where Jenny was sitting. He noticed that she wasn't wearing a wedding ring. "Madam, may I sit with you for a while?" He wanted to arrange her cutlery, napkin, and water cup in the same way as his own, but he controlled himself and didn't do it.
[29] "What? Oh, sure." Her tone of voice indicated that she didn't care much about it. She only cared about the door of the coffee shop. She still huddled in her chair and kept turning around to look at the door.
[30] "I'm glad to introduce myself to you. My name is... ah... " Poirot suddenly stopped. If he told her his name, the Chickadee and other waiters would also hear it, and he would no longer be the anonymous "foreign gentleman." He was a retired European police officer named Hercule Poirot, who had considerable influence among some people. For the past few weeks, he had been in a pleasant state of hibernation, which was the first time in his life that he had experienced being an ordinary person.
[31] It was obvious that Jinnie was not interested in his name or his arrival. A tear rolled down her cheek, trickling down her face.
[32] "Jinnie, Madame." Poirot hoped that calling her by her name would get her attention. He continued, "I used to be a policeman, now retired. During my time on the job, I met many people like you who were anxious and uneasy. There are many such people in every country, and I'm not saying they're not happy; they just feel they're in danger."
[33] Finally, he succeeded. Jinnie raised her terrified eyes and looked at Poirot. "Polic...e?" she said.
[34] "Yes, retired many years ago, but..."
[35] "So, you can't do anything in London, right? You can't... I mean, you don't have the right to do things like arresting criminals or anything like that, do you?"
[36] "Correct," Poirot says with a smile. "In London, I am just an old man enjoying his retirement."
[37] She hasn't looked at the door for over ten seconds.
[38] "Madam, did I say it right? Do you feel that you are in danger now? You keep turning your head to look outside, suspecting that the person you fear might follow you here and come in at any moment?"
[39] "Oh, yes, I am in danger." She seems about to say something else, but then asks, "Are you sure you're not a police officer?"
[40] "Not a police officer at all," Poirot affirms. But he doesn't want her to think he has no influence, so he continues, "I have a friend who is a detective with Scotland Yard. If you need help, I can contact him. He is young, around thirty, and I believe he will soon grow in the police force. I assure you, he will be happy to talk to you. As for me, I can—" The round-faced waiter approaches with a cup of tea, so Poirot doesn't continue.
[41] The round-faced waitress placed Jane's tea on the table and retreated to the kitchen. The curly-haired woman also returned to the kitchen. Poirot knew the curly-haired woman well. She loved gossiping about the customers who came to the cafe. Poirot imagined that his gentlemanly appearance and sudden approach to Jane would have been described in great detail in the kitchen. Unless he was having dinner with his friend Edward Catchpole, Poirot usually didn't talk much to the customers at the cafe. Edward Catchpole was a detective with the Metropolitan Police and was currently renting the same apartment as Poirot. He was like an animal in the French film "Winter Sleep," preferring to go his own way.
[42] Poirot didn't care what the coffee shop's waitresses were gossiping about in the kitchen. Instead, he was grateful that they had left. He hoped that with them gone, Jane could talk to him more openly. He continued, "Madam, I am willing to help you."
[43] "You're a good man, but no one can help me." Jane wiped her tears. "I really need help, more than anything. But it's too late. Look, I'm already dead, or about to be. I can't hide forever."
[44] The words "already dead" filled the cafe with a chill once again.
[45] "You see, no one can help me," she continued. "Even if they could, I wouldn't deserve it. But...with you sitting here, I feel a little better. I feel more comfortable." She crossed her arms around her shoulders, perhaps to sit more comfortably or to stop her body from shaking. Unfortunately, she couldn't control it. She didn't drink any tea, just kept saying, "Please don't leave. Nothing happened while I was talking to you. At least, it comforts me a little."
[46] "Mademoiselle, that is what worries me the most. You are still alive. To keep you alive, we must take action. Please tell me what happened."
[47] "No!" She widened her eyes and retreated back into her chair. "No, you can't do that! There's nothing you can do. It's inevitable. This is the only way justice can be served. I must die."
[48] Poirot furrowed his brow. Since he had sat down, Jean seemed to feel better, but he felt increasingly worse. So he said, "I don't understand what you mean. Are you saying someone is after you, trying to kill you?"
[49] Jean looked tearfully at Poirot and said, "If I just surrender and let it happen, can it still be considered murder? I'm tired, exhausted from running and hiding. Fear has worn me out. It's best to just let it happen. It's what I deserve."
[50] "That won't do," Poirot said. "I cannot agree with your idea until I understand your specific situation. Killing is never acceptable. Friend, you must let the police help you."
[51] "No. You can't tell him, or anyone. Swear you won't tell anyone."
[52] Poirot never promises to do something he can't do.
[53] "What did you do that you want to be killed as punishment? Did you kill someone?"
[54] "It's almost the same. You know, not only killing is unforgivable. I think you've never done anything unforgivable, right?"
[55] "Did you do it? And must you pay with your life? No. That's not right. I advise you to come with me to my residence, just nearby. Inspector Cader, from Scotland Yard…"
[56] "No." Jane jumped up from her chair.
[57] "Sit down, lady."
[58] "No. Ah, I'm so foolish! I've told you so much! I thought you looked kind and wouldn't do anything. If you hadn't said you're a retired policeman and a foreigner, I wouldn't have told you a word. You swear, if anyone finds out I'm dead, you'll tell your police friend not to look for the killer."
[59] She closed her eyes and clenched her hands together tightly. "Please, don't let them speak! Never solve the case. Promise me you'll tell your police friend what I said. Can you make him agree to that? If you care about justice, do as I say." She rushed towards the door and disappeared into the night. Poirot stood up, but as he rose from his chair, she was already gone, and he sat back down, sighing. Jane was gone, lost in the darkness, and he couldn't catch up to her.
[60] The kitchen door opened, and a woman with a tousled head served Poirot dinner, but the smell made him feel nauseous, and he had no appetite left.
[61] "Where's Jenny?" The bird's nest head asked Poirot, as if Jenny's sudden disappearance was his fault. He felt responsible for it. If he had been faster, if his words had been more careful...
[62] "I can't stand it!" The bird's nest head slammed Poirot's dinner on the table and turned around, walking back to the kitchen with big steps, shouting as he pushed the door, "Jenny left, and she didn't even pay the bill!"
[63] "Why would she risk her life for this?" Poirot murmured to himself.
[64] Poirot tried to eat the steak with Italian pasta and custard, but he had no interest. After a while, he knocked on the kitchen door. The bird's nest head opened the door a crack, his skinny body blocking the view completely.
[65] "Sir, is there anything wrong with your dinner?"
[66] "I would like to pay Jane's tea bill," Poirot suggested, "in exchange for a few questions."
[67] "Do you know Jane? I've never seen you two together before."
[68] "No, I don't. That's why I want to ask you some questions."
[69] "Then why did you sit with her?"
[70] "She was scared and upset, but I couldn't see why. I thought I could help her."
[71] "Jean is beyond help," the tousle-haired woman said. "All right, I'll answer your questions, but I have to ask you one first. Where did you used to be a police officer?"
[72] Polo didn't point out that she had already asked three questions, this was the fourth.
[73] She narrowed her small eyes at Polo and said, "Some people speak French but aren't French, right? I noticed your facial expression changed when those ladies called you 'Frenchy.'"
[74] Polo smiled and thought telling her his name wouldn't be dangerous, so he extended his hand and said, "Lady, my name is Hercule Poirot, from Belgium. It's nice to meet you."
[75] She shook Polo's hand and said, "I'm Phyllis Sprung. Actually, my name is Euphemia, but everyone calls me Phyllis. If they use my full name, they don't know what to say to me, and I don't like it."
[76] "Do you know what Jenny's full name is?"
[77] Fay nodded in the direction where Poirot had been sitting and saw the plate still steaming, so she said, "Have your dinner first, I'll be right out." She disappeared with a swoosh and closed the door.
[78] Poirot returned to his seat. Perhaps he should follow Fay Spurling's advice and try the steak dinner again. It was refreshing to chat with a meticulous person, and Poirot rarely encountered such a person.
[79] Fay came back quickly, holding a cup without a saucer. She sat down in the chair where Jenny had just been, took a sip of tea. Poirot's face twitched involuntarily at the sound of her sipping tea.
[80] "I don't know much about Jenny," she said, "but I've heard she worked as a maid for a wealthy family in a big house. So, whenever her mistress was going to have a lavish dinner or a party, she would come here to buy coffee and cake. She said it was a long walk from her house to here, but we have many customers who come from far away to buy coffee. Jenny always has a cup of tea whenever she comes. She always says, 'As usual, thank you,' as if she were a lady. I guess she's trying to make herself seem noble, and her voice is fake, not natural. So she doesn't talk much because she knows she can't keep it up for long."
[81] "Excuse me, Poirot," "How do you know that the voice of Miss Jenny is faked?"
[82] "Have you ever heard a maid speak like that? At least I haven't."
[83] "But...that's just your speculation."
[84] Fay Spence reluctantly admitted that she couldn't be sure, as Jenny had spoken like a "lady" since they met.
[85] "I would like to say something for Jenny. She knows how to appreciate tea, so she at least has some taste."
[86] "Tea?"
[87] "Yes." Phil looked at Polly's coffee cup and snorted. "If you must know, I can only say that when you coffee drinkers learn to enjoy tea, you'll know its worth."
[88] "Do you know where Mrs. Jennings works for a lady, or where that big house is?" Polly asked.
[89] "I don't know. I don't even know what Mrs. Jennings' last name is. I only know that she was deeply hurt years ago. That's what she said herself."
[90] "Hurt? Did she tell you what kind of harm it was?"
[91] "Just one thing." Phryne said firmly. "That's what broke her heart."
[92] "I mean, harm can be caused by many reasons, such as unrequited love or the untimely death of the one you love -"
[93] "Oh, they don't know the details." Phryne continued, with a hint of resentment in her voice. "She won't say, never. She only says that the hurt has been with her all along. That's just Janie, she never says much. Even if she were sitting here right now, you couldn't help her, and she's already gone. She lives in her own world, that's Janie's problem. She never talks about anything that happens."
[94] The phrase "living in her own world" suddenly reminded Poirot of something - a conversation he had with Phryne a few weeks earlier on a Thursday evening at the "Happy Coffee House."
[95] So Poirot continued, "She doesn't ask people questions either, right? Not interested in socializing or chatting, or caring about other people's gossip?"
[96] "Absolutely right," Phoebe exclaimed. "She has no curiosity. I've never seen anyone so self-absorbed, with no one else or the world in her eyes. She doesn't ask how others are doing or what they're up to."
[97] "I learned all that from your conversations with the other server, ma'am," Poirot said.
[98] Phoebe's face flushed. "I'm surprised you would listen to our conversations."
[99] To avoid making Phoebe more uncomfortable, Poirot didn't tell her that he had actually been looking forward to hearing her talk about other clients and had even speculated about them. These people could be considered "typical characters" at the "Joyful Coffee Shop," such as "Mr. Unsure," who always came in, ordered food, but then canceled his order because he wasn't really hungry.
[100] If the timing were right, Poirot would have liked to ask if Phoebe had given him a nickname like "Mr. Unsure" behind his back, perhaps something related to his neatly trimmed mustache.
[101] "So, Miss Jennings has no interest in others," Poirot mused. "Unlike others who may not like to pry into the lives and thoughts of those around them but indulge in lengthy self-praise – she's not like that, is she?"
[102] Phyllida raised an eyebrow. "You've got a good memory, and you're right. Miss Jennings doesn't like to talk about herself, but she'll answer questions without saying much. She doesn't like to dwell on anything. She has her own little secrets – ones that make her unhappy but keep her entangled. I gave up trying to figure her out a long time ago."
[103] "She's ensnared in hurt and danger," Poirot murmured.
[104] "Did she say she was in danger?"
[105] "Yes, madam. Unfortunately, I was too slow to stop her from leaving. If something had happened to her…" Poirot shook his head, trying to dispel the growing sense of foreboding. "I'll be back tomorrow morning, you said she often comes here, right? I'll find her before the danger arrives. This time, Hercule Poirot will be swift."
[106] "Speed doesn't matter," said Phryne. "The problem is that no one can find Madame Jenny. Even if she's right in front of you, you won't find her. No one can help her. She stood up, picked up Poirot's plate, and concluded with, "It's a shame to let such good food go cold."
[107] * * *
[108] Chapter 2
[109] Three Rooms of Murder
[110] The original text is in French. The French parts of the book are indicated in italics.
[111] It began like this: It was Thursday, February 7, 1929, and the characters were Hercule Poirot, Jeanne, and Phryne Sprynge, in the crooked, teapot-filled "Café Joyeux."
[112] Or I should say, it seemed like the beginning. I don't believe there's an actual beginning or end to real-life events. From any angle, you can trace the beginning of things back to a very long time ago, and then they continue on into the distant future. No one can say definitively, "That's where it starts," and draw a line in the sand.
[113] Fortunately, real stories always have a hero and a heroine. There won't be just me, and there won't be just one person, and I know for certain that those people are real.
[114] That Thursday evening, I wasn't in the café. Although my name was mentioned - Edward Catchpole, Poirot's friend in Scotland Yard, thirty-two years old (to be precise, thirty-two) - I wasn't there. To fill in the gaps where I wasn't, I felt it necessary and appropriate to record Jeanne's story. Luckily, I have Poirot's testimony, the best witness.
[115] I wrote these not for anyone else, but for myself. After each case, I read them again and again until I could read them with a calm mind, without the initial shock of "How could this happen?" to "Yes, this is what happened."
[116] In a way, I should have come up with a better title than "Jean's Story." It doesn't sound like a title.
[117] I met Hercule Poirot only six weeks before the Thursday night mentioned above. He was planning to rent Mrs. Blanche's apartment. The little house was spacious and spotlessly clean, with a square exterior and an interior that was as feminine as possible: full of lace, ruffles, and trimmings. Sometimes I even worried that when I left for work, I would suddenly find a lavender tassel hanging from something in the living room, wrapped around my arm or shoe.
[118] Unlike me, Poirot did not plan to stay there permanently, only temporarily. That evening, he told me, "I'm here to relax and rest, at least for a month." He spoke firmly, as if he were afraid I would interrupt him. "My brain works too fast," he explained to me, "Thoughts come rushing in... I believe I can relax here."
[119] When I asked him where he was from, I thought he would say "France." It was only later that I found out he was Belgian, not French. At the time, he walked over to the window, pulled back the lace curtains, and pointed to a large, beautiful house about 300 yards away as an answer to the question. "You live there?" I asked, thinking he was joking.
[120] "Yes, I hope not too far away," Poirot said. "It's even better that I can see it every day: what a beautiful view!" He proudly gazed at the grand mansion. For a while, I even wondered if he had forgotten that I was still standing next to him. Then he went on to say, "Travel is also a pleasant thing! Exciting, but not restful. But if I don't go out, Poirot's brain won't get a vacation! Worry will follow. It's easy to be found at home, whether by friends or strangers, always with very important things - always very important! - and my gray brain cells have to work again, without having the chance to store energy. So, I tell people that Poirot is leaving London for a while, but in fact, he found a familiar place to rest and be undisturbed."
[121] As he spoke, I nodded along, as if everything sounded perfectly reasonable. I wondered if people just got stranger as they got older.
[122] On Thursday night, Mrs. Eversleigh never cooked, and she went to her late husband's sister's house. So Poirot had to go to "The Happy Café." He told me that during his time away, he didn't want to risk going to his usual places and asked me, "My friend, can you recommend a place like yours, with good food?" I recommended "The Happy Café": a small, odd place, but one that you would want to return to after visiting once.
[123] On that particular Thursday night - the night Poirot and Jenny met, he came back at ten past ten, much later than usual. I sat by the fireplace in the living room, but I didn't feel warm at all. I heard the front door open and close, and then Mrs. Eversleigh and Poirot whispered to each other for a while. She must have been waiting for him in the hallway.
[124] I couldn't hear what she said, but I could guess: she was anxious, and the reason for her anxiety was me. She had noticed something was wrong with me when she came back from her sister's house at nine thirty. I looked a little scary, like I hadn't eaten or slept in a while. She told me so directly. But I didn't know how someone could suddenly look like they hadn't eaten, maybe I was a little thinner than when I had breakfast.
[125] At the time, she examined me from head to toe, from left to right, and then brought out everything she could think of to make me feel better - food, drinks, and even chatted with me. I politely refused her kindness, and she immediately suggested some strange things: a pillow stuffed with herbs, and a weird-smelling but supposedly effective thing in a deep blue bottle that I had to put in the bathwater.
[126] I thanked her and declined her offers. She looked around the living room, trying to find something to force on me that could solve my problem, but she couldn't find anything.
[127] Now, she was whispering to Poirot, probably trying to convince him to force me to accept her stinky blue bottle or herb pillow.
[128] On Thursday evenings, Poirot usually returned from "The Joyful Cafe" at nine. Tonight, I returned from the Blaxland Hotel at nine fifteen, determined not to think about what had happened there and hoping for the usual routine of watching Poirot sitting in his favorite chair, chatting about trivial matters.
[129] But he wasn't there. It left me feeling empty, as if the sky had fallen. Poirot was a man of routine, and he rarely deviated from it. He had once told me, "Carter, it's the monotony of this routine that keeps one calm." But tonight, he was an hour and fifteen minutes late.
[130] At nine thirty, I heard the sound of the front door opening, thinking it was him, but it was Mrs. U.N. Owen. I almost complained out loud. If you're anxious, the last thing you need is a companion who is not cheerful and overly concerned.
[131] I couldn't convince myself to go back to the Brawnsham Hotel tomorrow, but I knew I had to. And that was the last thing I wanted to think about right now.
[132] Now, Poirot was back, and I knew he would be worried about me, as Mrs. Bunscombe had informed him. I believed that I would be more comfortable without them around. Since there was no opportunity for any light-hearted conversation today, I decided to say nothing.
[133] Poirot entered the living room still wearing his coat and hat, closing the door. I expected him to have a long list of questions, but he seemed somewhat carelessly said, "It's quite late. I was out looking for a woman, Jane, hoping she's still alive and hasn't been murdered. I couldn't find anything."
[134] He was worried, yes, but not about me or whether I had eaten or wanted to eat. This made me feel relieved. "Looking?" I asked.
[135] "Yes, I was looking for a woman, Jane, hoping she's still alive and hasn't been murdered."
[136] "Murdered?" I feel the ground give way beneath me again. Poirot is a famous detective, and he's told me about some of the cases he's worked on. That's why I didn't intend to tell him about what I'm doing, since he wanted to take a break from those cases, but now he's said a word that makes me uneasy.
[137] "What does she look like?" I ask him. "Tell me about her. Maybe I've seen her somewhere. Especially if she's been murdered. I saw three people killed tonight, two women and a man, maybe it's the one you're talking about. The man didn't look like Jane, but the two women…"
[138] "My friend," Poirot interrupts me, his voice calm, and the despairing words have no effect. He takes off his hat and begins to unbutton his coat. "Mrs. Blanche says you have a problem. Ha! I didn't notice. Tell Poirot what's happened."
[139] "Three people were killed," I say. "Completely different from the previous cases. Two women and a man died in different rooms."
[140] I've been a policeman for five years and have been working at Scotland Yard for nearly two years, and I've seen many violent deaths, most of which seemed to be caused by loss of control. Someone killed in a fit of rage, someone killed because of alcohol. The case at the Bluxton Hotel tonight is different, with three crimes committed in the same hotel on the same day. I believe that at least several months would have been needed to prepare for this. Each crime scene is a chilling work of art that I can't decipher. What frightens me is that this time I can't crack this cold, carefully planned murder with the methods I usually use against hooligans and thugs.
[141] "Undoubtedly, I'm upset now, but it won't change my theory," I thought. When I think about the three bodies arranged the same way, I shiver. I tell myself to handle the case normally, regardless of what it looks like on the surface, and not to be afraid.
[142] "Is it the same three rooms in the same house?" Polo asked.
[143] "No, it's the Brawnsham Hotel, just a few steps from Piccadilly Circus. Do you know where it is?"
[144] "No."
[145] "I've never been there before, and it's not a place where someone like me can go. It's too luxurious."
[146] Poirot sat there, his back straight, muttering to himself, "Three murders, the same hotel, different rooms."
[147] "Yes, and all today, in the evening, and the crime scenes were cleared up quickly."
[148] "Tonight? But you're at home. Why aren't you at the hotel? Has the killer been caught?"
[149] "Unfortunately, not. I... I stopped talking and cleared my throat, deciding that it was enough to tell him the situation, without mentioning how much the crime scene affected my emotions or how I didn't want to stay at Bluxton Hotel for even five more minutes.
[150] The three bodies lay neatly on the floor: arms by their sides, palms down, legs together.
[151] "Lying on the ground, dead bodies," these words brought back memories of a dark room I was forced into when I was a child. From then on, I never wanted to remember that again and hoped never to remember it in my life.
[152] Weak hands, palms down. "Edward, hold his hand."
[153] "Don't worry, there are many policemen there." To distract myself from that unpleasant memory, I shouted, "I'll be back first thing tomorrow morning." Seeing Poirot waiting for further explanation, I added, "I need to clear my head. Honestly, I've never seen such a bizarre case."
[154] "How bizarre?"
[155] "Each victim, both of them, had the same thing in their mouths."
[156] "No." Poirot shook his finger. "My friend, this is impossible. The same thing cannot be in the mouths of three people at the same time."
[157] "Three identical ones." I explained, "Three cufflinks, they look like pure gold, with the same letter combination: PIJ. Poirot? Are you okay? You look -"
[158] "Goodness!" He stood up and began to pace around the room. "My friend, you don't understand what this means. Yes, you definitely don't understand, because you don't know about my encounter with Miss Jennings. I must tell you what happened tonight so you will understand."
[159] When Poirot said to tell the story quickly, he was different from most people. Every detail was important to him, whether it was hundreds of people dying in a fire or a small pit on a child's chin. No one could rush him to get to the point, so I sat firmly in my chair and listened to him tell the story in his own way. By the time he finished, I felt like I was at the scene of the crime - in fact, I knew more about it than when I was actually there.
[160] "How incredible!" I said. "It's too coincidental. Tonight, three murders occurred at the Brawnsham Hotel."
[161] Poirot sighed. "I don't think it's a coincidence, my friend. Coincidences do happen, but these events are clearly connected."
[162] "You mean one murder and fear of being murdered," I said.
[163] "No. That is another connection, but I mean other connections." Poirot suddenly stopped pacing and turned to me. "You said that the three victims had a gold cufflink with the letter combination PIJ in their mouths, right?"
[164] "Yes."
[165] "Miss Valentine clearly told me, 'You swear, if anyone finds me dead, you tell your police friend not to look for the killer. Don't let them talk! Never solve the case.' What do you think 'don't let them talk' means?"
[166] "Mrs. Vane clearly told me, 'You swear that if anyone finds me dead, you tell your policeman friend not to look for the killer. Don't let them talk! Never solve the case.' What do you think 'Don't let them talk' means?"
[167] Poirot seemed to be joking, but it wasn't. I said, "Well, it's pretty clear, isn't it? She was afraid of being killed and didn't want the killer to be punished. She thought she deserved it."
[168] "You only understood the literal meaning." Poirot said, sounding disappointed in my answer, "Ask yourself, 'Don't let them talk' has another meaning. Think about it from the perspective of your three gold cufflinks."
[169] "It's not mine." I emphasized, hoping to put the case aside, "Okay, I know what you mean, but..."
[170] "What do you know? What do I mean?"
[171] "Indeed! 'Please don't let anyone open their mouths, don't find the gold cufflink with the PIJ combination.' Isn't that what Jean meant? She knew there would be three guests killed in the hotel, and she knew the killer would kill her too?"
[172] Before I could answer, Poirot continued his imaginative thinking. "The letter combination PIJ, the initials of a person who is important to this case, right? Jean knew this. She also knew that once you discovered these three letters, you would trace back and find the killer. She didn't want you to do that. But you have to catch the killer, before Jean is killed, otherwise, Hercule Poirot will not be able to forgive himself!"
[173] Hearing this, I was shocked. Not only did I feel a strong sense of responsibility to catch the killer, but if Poirot could not forgive himself, I couldn't bear that responsibility. Does he think I have the ability to arrest the killer? The one who puts a gold cufflink with the initials in the mouths of the dead? I'm a straightforward person, so I can only handle things straightforwardly.
[174] "You must go back to the hotel," Poirot said. He meant immediately.
[175] Thinking of the three rooms, I shivered. I told him, "I'll go there tomorrow morning." I deliberately avoided his glowing eyes, "I must tell you, I'm not going to say such a thing about Jean, it will confuse others. You have your point of view, and I have mine. Your point of view is interesting, but my understanding sounds more like the truth."
[176] "No," Poirot countered.
[177] "We can disagree," I said firmly. "But if I asked a hundred people, I'm sure they would all be on my side."
[178] "I'm sure of that too," Poirot sighed. "If I could, I would prove it to you. When you were telling me about the hotel murders, you said, 'Every victim, he or she had the same thing in their mouths,' right?"
[179] I admitted it.
[180] "You said 'their mouths,' not 'his or her mouths.' You are well-educated, using the singular 'he or she' instead of the plural 'they,' to maintain consistency with 'everyone' and ensure grammatical correctness. Miss Jenny is a maid, but her speech and vocabulary suggest a good education. When she spoke of her own death and the killer who would kill her, she used the word 'inevitable.' She then said to me, 'You see, no one can help me, even if they could, I would feel guilty.' She was a very careful speaker. So, my friend…" Poirot resumed his pace, "So! If you are right, and Jenny meant 'do not let them speak' to mean 'do not let anyone speak to the police,' then why didn't she say 'do not let anyone speak'? 'Anyone' matches the singular, not the plural."
[181] I looked up at him, staring with wide eyes until my neck ached. I was puzzled and exhausted, with no energy to respond. Had he just told me that Miss Vera was extremely frightened? Based on my experience, a frightened person would not have the time to worry about grammar.
[182] I had always thought Poirot was wise, but now it seemed I may have misjudged him. If all this was true, it was no wonder he wanted to relax his mind.
[183] "Of course, you would say that Miss Vera was very upset and did not care about grammar at the time," Poirot continued speaking, "but, except for this one sentence, all her words were grammatically correct. Therefore, it must be that I am right and you are wrong, so this sentence of Miss Vera's has no grammatical issues."
[184] He clapped his hands, satisfied with his argument. I emphasized my words: "Very clever, Poirot. Three people were murdered, and it is my duty to find out the truth. But I am still glad that Miss Vera, whoever she was, spoke without any grammatical errors."
[185] "Poirot is also glad," my determined friend continued, "because we have made some progress and discovered something new. By the way," suddenly, his smile disappeared, and his tone became serious, "Miss Vera did not make any grammatical errors, so what she really meant was, 'please do not let anyone open the mouths of the three victims, that is, their mouths.'"
[186] "If you insist," I muttered.
[187] "Go to the Bluxton Hotel first thing after breakfast tomorrow," Poirot said. "I'll find Jenny first and then come to the hotel to meet you."
[188] "You too?" I said, a bit annoyed. I had already formulated a refusal in my mind, but I knew he wouldn't listen. Regardless of whether he's a famous detective or not, honestly, his current views on the case are too absurd. But since he offered to accompany me on the case, I shouldn't refuse. The reason is that he's confident, I'm not, and his interest in the case has given me some confidence.
[189] "That's right," he said. "There's an extremely unusual commonality among the three murders: each victim had a cufflink with an initial combination in their mouths. So, I'm definitely going to the Bluxton Hotel."
[190] "Weren't you trying to avoid stimulation and give your brain a rest?" I asked.
[191] "Yes, that's right," Poirot said, staring at me. "But how can I relax when I spend the whole day sitting in a chair, thinking that you won't mention the important details of my meeting with Miss Jenny? Besides, thinking that Miss Jenny is wandering around London, and the murderer could kill her at any time, and put the fourth cufflink in her mouth, I can't relax either."
[192] Poirot leaned forward and said, "Tell me you care about this too, don't the cufflinks always come in pairs? Three were put into the mouths of the three victims of the Brawnsham Hotel, and the fourth? If the fourth is not in the murderer's pocket, waiting to put it in Miss Jenny's mouth after killing her, where will it be?"
[193] I couldn't help laughing at Poirot and said, "Poirot, that's ridiculous. Yes, cufflinks are usually in pairs. But it's simple, the murderer only wanted to kill three people, so he only used three. You can't come up with the idea of the fourth cufflink and prove something - you can't connect the murder case in the hotel with Miss Jenny."
[194] Poirot stubbornly said to me, "My friend, if you were the killer, you would have thought of the cufflinks being in pairs when deciding to use them as a means of committing the crime. It was the killer who came up with the idea of the fourth cufflink and the fourth victim, not Hercule Poirot!"
[195] "But... how do we know he didn't have the idea of six or eight victims? Who dares say that the murderer's pocket won't have five more cufflinks with the initial combination PIJ?"
[196] To my horror, this time Poirot nodded and said, "That's a good point."
[197] "No, Poirot, that's not a good point," I said dejectedly. "I was just saying it as a joke, with no basis. You may like my imaginative thinking, but I can assure you, my Scotland Yard superiors won't."
[198] "Your superiors don't like you thinking about multiple possibilities? Oh, they certainly don't," Poirot answered himself. "They're just responsible for catching the killer, just like you. Well! So, Hercule Poirot must go to the Blaukenshaw Hotel tomorrow."

[200] Approximately 270 meters.
[201] Chapter 3
[202] At the Browns Hotel
[203] The next morning, at the Browns Hotel, I felt uneasy knowing Polo could appear at any moment. He would surely criticize our methods for handling the three murder cases. Only I knew he would come, so I was even more nervous. His presence would be my responsibility, and I feared his words would demoralize the team. To be honest, I was afraid he would disappoint me. It was a rare sunny day in February, and the pleasant weather lifted my spirits, but I couldn't understand why I didn't stop Polo from approaching the hotel or any nearby places.
[204] However, I didn't expect stopping him would make a difference; even if I tried, he wouldn't listen.
[205] When Polo arrived, I was conversing with the hotel manager, Luca Lazari. Lazari was a kind, helpful, and enthusiastic man with black curly hair, a gentle voice, and a mustache just like Polo's. Lazari believed that, as police officers working at the Browns Hotel, we should enjoy our stay, just like the other guests - those who haven't been murdered.
[206] I introduced him to Poirot, who only nodded slightly, looking rather downcast. I soon understood what was going on. "I couldn't find Jean," he said. "I waited for her in the café for most of the morning, but she never came."
[207] "Not most of the morning, Poirot," I said, thinking he was exaggerating.
[208] "And Miss Fay is not here either. I asked the other waiters, but they know nothing."
[209] "Bad luck," I said. His words didn't surprise me, as I had always felt that Jean would not go back to that café. I felt a little guilty for not telling him my thoughts earlier. Perhaps I should have made it clear to Poirot yesterday: since Jean had run away from him, left the Happy Café, and admitted that revealing her secret to him was a mistake, why did she go back to him for protection the next day?
[210] "Yes!" Poirot looked at me expectantly. "Do you have anything to tell me?"
[211] At this point, Lazari smiled and said, "I'm Luca Lazari, here to provide you with information at your service, Poirot sir. Have you ever been to the Blaux Han Hotel before?"
[212] "No."
[213] "Not luxurious enough for you? Like a palace from the 'Golden Age,' isn't it? Majestic and grand! I hope you've noticed and appreciated the artworks around you!"
[214] "Yes, much more luxurious than Mrs. Blanche Onsworth's apartment, but the view is better there," Poirot said sarcastically, his gloomy mood clearly getting the better of him.
[215] "Ah, the sights from our hotel rooms!" Lazari exclaimed, clasping his hands together excitedly. "From the rooms facing the hotel garden, you can see a beautiful view; on the other side, there's the great city of London, with its own unique charm! I'll show you later."
[216] "I'd rather see the three crime scenes," Poirot told Lazari.
[217] Lazari's smile froze for a moment as Poirot spoke. "Mr. Poirot, please rest assured that such a terrible crime will never happen again at the world-renowned Blauw-Han Hotel. Three people killed in one night? I can hardly believe it!"
[218] Poirot and I exchanged a glance, both knowing that the focus now was not on preventing future occurrences, but on dealing with what had already happened.
[219] I noticed Poirot struggling to control his anger, with his mustache twitching. Deciding to regain control, I interrupted Lazari before he could continue.
[220] "The three victims were Mrs. Harriet Sipper, Miss Edith Gransbury, and Mr. Richard Nigges, all guests of the hotel and each with their own rooms," I told Poirot. "They were all killed alone in their rooms."
[221] "Each?" Poirot joked, then smiled. I believe his mood improved because Lazari stopped talking.
[222] "They all checked in on Tuesday, the day before the murders."
[223] "Were they together?"
[224] "No."
[225] "Absolutely not," Lazari spoke up again. "They arrived one by one, each checking in separately."
[226] "And one by one they were killed." Poirot said, just as I was thinking the same thing. He asked Lazari again, "Are you sure?"
[227] "I couldn't be more certain. I have another employee who can vouch for me, Mr. John Good, the most reliable person I know. You'll know him, Poirot. The Brouxes Hotel only hires the best. So I believe everything my employees say. People come from all over the country, all over the world to apply for a job at the Brouxes Hotel, and I only pick the best."
[228] Ironically, it was only now, watching Lazari's flustered expression, that I realized how well I knew Poirot. Even if Lazari had a sign around John Good's neck saying "suspect," it wouldn't have done more to arouse Poirot's suspicions. Hercule Poirot would never allow anyone to impose their opinion on him; he would rather believe the opposite. He was a stubborn old man who liked to argue with people.
[229] So Poirot said, "So, it's a remarkable coincidence, isn't it? Mrs. Harriet Sipper, Miss Ada Gransbury, and Mr. Richard Nix, the three victims arrived separately, with no apparent connection. Not only did they share the same day of death, yesterday, but they all chose to check into the Brouxes Hotel on Tuesday."
[230] "What's so remarkable about that?" I asked him. "Such a large hotel, there must have been many guests who checked in on Tuesday, but they weren't killed."
[231] Poirot's eyes bulged out of his head as if he was about to jump out of it. I didn't think I said anything particularly, so I pretended not to notice his terror and continued with the case. [232]
[232] "Each victim was found in their bedroom, with the door locked." I found myself emphasizing "he or she." "The killer locked the door and took the key with them –" [233]
[233] "Wait a minute," Poirot interrupted me suddenly. "You mean the key is missing, but we can't be sure if it was the killer who took it, or if it's still in their possession?" [234]
[234] I took a deep breath and said, "We suspect the killer took the key. We searched the room thoroughly and didn't find it, nor anywhere else in the hotel." [235]
[235] "My excellent employees checked, and it's true," Lazari said.
[236] Poirot said he wanted to conduct a thorough search of the three rooms himself, and Lazari agreed readily, as if Poirot were proposing a tea party, followed by a dance.

[238] "Perhaps he put it in his pocket, along with one, or two, or three, or five cufflinks," Poirot said coldly.

[242] "Yes, the teacups and the glass Mr. Nigges used are still in the rooms. Although there are only a few drops of liquid left, it's easy to tell that it's tea and not coffee. I'm sure we'll find cyanide in there."

[244] "According to the forensic examination, all three victims died between 4 PM and 8:30 PM. Fortunately, we have narrowed down the time to between 7:15 PM and 8:20 PM."

[247] "Wait a minute," Poirot said, "we'll get to that note. Mr. Razali, each of the victims was seen by a hotel employee at exactly 7:15 PM. How is that possible?"
[248] "Yes, that's right." Razali nodded vigorously, I was worried his head might fall off, "Absolutely true. All three guests ordered food and requested it to be delivered to their rooms at 7:15 PM, and all three were delivered on time. That's the principle of the Browns Hotel."
[249] Poirot turned to me and said, "Another unusual coincidence. Harriet Sipper, Ada Gransbury, and Richard Nigges checked into the same hotel on the same day and were all killed the next day. And all three requested their dinner to be delivered to their rooms at 7:15 PM on the day they died? That's impossible."
[250] "Poirot, the fact is that it has already happened, and arguing about the possibility of certain things has no meaning," I said.

[252] "Let's hurry, then, before they remove the bodies," I said. "Normally, they would have been removed by now." I didn't mention that the delay in removing the bodies was due to my negligence. Last night, I was so eager to leave the Blaxland Hotel and was preoccupied with other thoughts that I forgot to arrange for the necessary actions.
[253] I thought that Lazari's departure would improve Poirot's mood, but his cold and determined demeanor remained unchanged. I finally understood that this was his "work state." However, this was my job, not his, so I felt he was going too far and didn't boost my determination.
[254] I took a universal key and prepared to accompany him to the rooms of the three victims. Standing in front of the elaborately decorated golden elevator, Poirot said to me, "There is one thing I hope we agree on: Mr. Lazari's words about the hotel staff cannot be trusted. It seems he thinks his employees are all above suspicion, but if they were all in the hotel yesterday when the crime occurred, they would all be under suspicion. Mr. Lazari's loyalty to his work is commendable, but if he thinks all the employees in the hotel are angels, then he is a fool."
[255] I suddenly became impatient and decided to speak my mind: "I hope you don't think I'm a fool too. I said there were many guests who checked in on Wednesday... I was too hasty in saying that. The guests who checked in on Wednesday but did not die the following day have nothing to do with it, right? My point is, only when three or more seemingly unrelated guests check in on the same day and are murdered on the same night can it be considered a significant coincidence."
[256] "Yes." Poirot said with a sincere smile as he stepped into the elevator, "You have finally regained your sharp mind. My judgment was not mistaken. You said 'seemingly unrelated' and you hit the nail on the head. Now I can assure you that these three victims must have had a connection. They were not randomly selected from the hotel guests. The reason for their deaths is the same - all related to the initials PIJ. And the reason they checked in on the same day at the same hotel is the same."
[257] "Like an invitation to come and die." I said casually, "The invitation said, 'Please arrive a day in advance so that you can enjoy death on Thursday.'"
[258] Joking about this may not be appropriate, but when I'm feeling down, I can only make jokes. Sometimes I can even fool myself into thinking everything is still okay. However, this time it didn't work.
[259] "Enjoy... " Poirot muttered, "Yes, my friend, that's exactly what I mean. I know you were just joking, but you hit the nail on the head and it's quite interesting."
[260] I don't agree, it was just a stupid joke with no other meaning. But Poirot seemed to genuinely appreciate my absurd idea.
[261] "One, two, three," Poirot murmured as the elevator ascended. "Harriet Spear stays on the 121, Richard Niggles on the 238, and Ada Gransbury on the 317. These three victims are on consecutive floors. It's quite organized, isn't it?" Poirot usually appreciates order, but this time, the organized arrangement seemed to unsettle him.
[262] We inspected three nearly identical rooms. Each had a bed, a wardrobe, a washbasin, a glass cup facing upwards on the sink, several armchairs, a small coffee table, a desk, a tiled fireplace, a radiator, a large desk in front of the window, a suitcase, some clothes, and personal items, as well as a corpse.
[263] All the doors slammed shut, trapping me inside…
[264] "Grab his hand, Edward."
[265] I dared not get too close to the bodies. They lay rigidly on the ground, feet facing the door, arms flat on their sides. Neatly arranged.
[266] (Just writing these sentences describing the corpse's posture was already unbearable. It's no wonder I couldn't bear to look at the three corpses up close, only for a few seconds at a time? The bluish skin, the stiff, swollen tongue, the wrinkled lips. Indeed, I shouldn't have focused on the lifeless hands, but rather examined their faces closely; I shouldn't have been lost in thought, but I couldn't help it: I wondered if Harriet Sipper, Ada Gransbury, and Richard Nigg would have wanted someone to hold their hands after death, or if they would have been horrified by the idea. Goodness, the human brain is a fickle and uncontrollable organ. Just thinking about it made me feel miserable.)
[267] Neatly…
[268] Suddenly, an idea seized me. The crime scenes were too strange. I realized that the way the bodies were arranged resembled the way doctors position patients who have been treated for months but ultimately die. The bodies of Harriet Sipper, Ada Gransbury, and Richard Nigg had been meticulously arranged - at least, that's how it seemed to me. The killer had taken care of them after the murder, which suggested that he was cold-blooded and chilling.
[269] Just as I had this thought, I dismissed it. This place doesn't provide such services. I had mixed past and present, mixing what happened at the Blakeshan Hotel with my most unpleasant childhood memories. I commanded myself not to be distracted and focused on the matter at hand, trying to see the facts through Pollock's eyes without the distortion of my own life experiences.
[270] Each victim lay on the floor between a wingback armchair and a small coffee table. In Harriet Sipper and Ada Gransbury's rooms, there was a teacup with a saucer on the small coffee table; in Richard Nigg's room, there was only a sherry glass. In Ada Gransbury's 317 room, there was a tray on the larger table by the window, with a set of teacups and saucers, an empty teacup, and a plate with some crumbs, but nothing else.
[271] "Ah ha," said Poirot, "there are two tea cups and many plates in this room, and it's clear that someone had dinner with Miss Ada Gransberry. It's probably the killer. But why is the tray still in the room? When were the trays taken away from the rooms of Harriet Sipper and Richard Nigges?"
[272] "They might not have ordered anything," I said. "They might have just ordered drinks - tea and sherry - so there was no tray to begin with. And Ada Gransberry brought a lot of clothes, twice as many as the other two. Look, there's not even room for another petticoat in the wardrobe."
[273] "You're right," said Poirot. "Lazari said they all ordered dinner, and we'll check later what each of them specifically ordered. I wouldn't have overlooked this if I hadn't been worried about Jenny. I still don't know where Jenny is! And Jenny is about the same age as these three victims, between forty and forty-five years old."
[274] While Poirot checked their mouths and cufflinks, I turned my face away. He checked them, occasionally exclaiming in various ways. I looked at the fireplace and out the window from time to time, trying to avoid thinking again about the hands that would never have to be held again. I even thought about my crossword puzzle, where I had made a mistake.
[275] After checking the three rooms, Poirot insisted on returning to the third floor, room 238, where Richard Nigges was. Perhaps going to the rooms of the victims so many times won't make me feel uncomfortable? So far, the answer is still no. As I entered Nigges' room again, it felt like I was being forced to climb a treacherous mountain, and I knew beforehand that reaching the summit would lead to disaster.
[276] Poirot did not notice my distress, and I hoped it was because I had covered it up and not attracted his attention. It's cold in Mr. Nix's room!"
[277] "Lazari found the body in this room just like this," I said, "without any changes."

[279] I told him I had seen them, wondering if he thought I was an idiot for not seeing the trees right by the window.
[280] "Another difference in this room is the position of the cufflinks," said Poirot. "Did you notice? Miss Harriet Sippel and Miss Edith Garnet's cufflinks were in their mouths, slightly protruding from their lips. While Richard Nix's cufflinks were very deep inside, almost at his throat."
[281] I opened my mouth to object, then gave up, but it was too late, Poirot saw the denial in my eyes. So he asked, "What do you want to say?"
[282] "I think you're being too rigid," I said. "The three victims all had a cufflink with the initials PIJ in their mouths, which is a common point, not a different one. The position of the cufflink in the mouth is not a big issue."
[283] "This is a huge difference! The lips and throat are different positions, completely different!" Poirot came over to me and stood in front of me, continuing, "Katchuk, please remember this: when three murders are almost identical, every tiny detail is crucial."
[284] Do I really have to remember this pearl of wisdom, even though I don't agree with it? However, Poirot need not worry, I remember everything he has said to me, word for word, and the more he angers me, the better I remember.
[285] "All three victims had cufflinks in their mouths," I repeated stubbornly, "and that's enough for me."
[286] "I understand," Poirot said with disappointment. "It's enough for you, and it's probably enough for the hundreds of people under you, and I have no doubt it's enough for your superiors and Scotland Yard. But for Hercule Poirot, it's not enough!"
[287] I had to remind myself that Poirot was talking about the similarities and differences in the case, not me.
[288] "What about the open windows?" he asked me. "The windows in the other two rooms were closed. Is that not important?"
[289] "Maybe it doesn't matter," I said. "Richard Nigges might have opened this room's window himself, and there was no reason for the killer to close it. Poirot, you often say that even in the winter, British people like to open windows because we believe it's good for the body."
[290] "My friend," Poirot said patiently, "consider this: it is impossible for the three people not to have been poisoned, to have fallen naturally from the armchair and lie flat on the floor with their hands by their sides and their feet pointing towards the door. Why didn't anyone crawl across the room? Why didn't anyone fall on the other side of the chair? The killer rearranged their bodies to ensure that each corpse was in the same position, at the same distance from the chair and the small table. So, since the killer went to such lengths to make the three crime scenes identical, why didn't he close this room's window too? Perhaps Richard Nigges opened the window himself, but why didn't the killer close it to keep it consistent with the other two rooms' windows?"
[291] I had to think about this. Poirot was right; the bodies had been arranged intentionally to make them look identical.
[292] The bodies arranged neatly...
[293] "I think it depends on where you draw the line for the crime scene," I said, trying to pull myself back to the darkest room I had ever experienced. "It depends on whether you include the window or not."
[294] "Draw the line for the crime scene?"
[295] "Yes, not literally, but theoretically. Perhaps, the killer only created a small crime scene here."

[992] I nodded, a bit fearful. What if I found out that all three victims were heartless villains when alive? "May they rest in peace" flashed through my mind, followed by a recollection of Poirot's description of his and Jane's meeting, Jane insisting that only her death would be fair...
[993] "Edith was a deadly egomaniac," Margaret said. "She was just as hypocritical as Harriet, but Edith did it out of fear and faith, not for the thrill of tormenting others. Unlike Harriet, condemning others' wrongdoings didn't bring Edith pleasure; she believed it was her duty as a devout Christian."
[994] "You mean fear of divine retribution?"
[995] "Oh, certainly, but not entirely," Margaret said. "People's views on rules are always different. I, for one, don't like being confined, even if some rules are perfectly reasonable. But others like and enforce them, feeling safer and more protected."
[996] "Is Ada Grantsbury one of them?"
[997] "Yes, I think so. But she wouldn't say so herself. She always acts cautiously, trying to appear as a principled woman. Ada has some human weaknesses, but nothing to be ashamed of! Despite doing many unknown harmful deeds while alive, I still find it hard to bear her death. Unlike Harriet, Ada believes in redemption. Ada wants to save sinners, while Harriet only wants to reprimand them and gain a sense of superiority. I believe Ada would forgive a sinner who truly wants to repent, and this belief supports her worldview."
[998] "What harmful deeds did Ada Grantsbury do that were unknown to others?" I ask her. "Who did she harm?"
[999] "Ask me that tomorrow," she says, her tone both confident and resolute.
[1000] "Was it Patrick and Francis Ives, the couple?"
[1001] "Tomorrow, Mr. Cuthbert."
[1002] "Can you tell me anything about Richard Nigg?" I asked next.
[1003] "I'm afraid I know very little. He left after Charles and I arrived in Gresholm. I think he had a lot of influence in the village, and people listened to him and sought his advice. Everyone spoke highly of him except Ada Gransby."
[1004] "Who decided to cancel the engagement?" I asked.
[1005] "He did."
[1006] "How do you know she hasn't mentioned him to anyone else? She might just not mention him in front of you, but what about others?"
[1007] "Oh, Ada doesn't mention Richard Nigg in front of me, nor does she mention him to anyone else. I was told this by Ambrose Flavard, the village doctor, the most reliable person in the world. As long as he leaves the clinic door half-open, he hears a lot."
[1008] "Is this the same Flavard doctor you just made me forget? I think I should forget his Christian name as well."
[1009] Margaret ignored my joke and continued, "I'm sure Ada decided not to mention him or even think about him after Richard left her. She seems not to be sad on the surface. People praise her for being strong and decisive. She even announced that from now on she will devote all her love to God, as she believes God is more reliable than mortals."
[1010] "Were you surprised to hear that Richard Nigg and Ada Gransby were having tea together in a hotel room in London last Thursday?"
[1011] Margaret widened her eyes and said, "Yes, I was really shocked when you told me they were together and having tea. Ada is the kind of person who draws a clear line and never crosses it. Richard Nixey is also like that, saying he won't marry Ada, and he won't change his mind. I think, unless he confesses and asks her again, Ada would never agree to meet him alone."
[1012] After a slight pause, Margaret continued, "Since Harriet Sipper also stayed at the same hotel, I guess she must have joined them for tea too, right?"
[1013] I nodded.
[1014] "Oh, so they must have had something very important to talk about, something more important than any line drawn in the past."
[1015] "You already know what they were talking about, don't you?"
[1016] Margaret looked out the window at the row of tombstones and said, "Maybe I'll have some ideas when you come tomorrow."

[1019] Chapter 11

[1078] Chapter 12
[1079] Painful Wound
[1080] The next day, after breakfast, I went to Margaret Ernest's cottage near the churchyard of St. Gregory's. The door was half open, and I gently knocked a few times to make sure I didn't push it open further.
[1081] No one answered, so I knocked again several times. "Mrs. Ernst? Margaret?" I called out loudly.
[1082] Silence.
[1083] For some reason, I felt something moving behind me, so I turned around, but it might have been just the sound of the wind blowing through the branches.
[1084] I gently pushed the door, and it creaked open. The first thing I saw was a scarf, a bright green silk one with intricate patterns, lying on the flagstone floor of the kitchen. How did it get here? I took a deep breath, and then a voice called out from inside, "Come in, Mr. Kitcher."
[1085] Margaret Ernst walked into the kitchen. "Oh, I was looking for it," she said with a smile, picking up the scarf and wrapping it around her neck. "I knew you'd come, so I left the door open. Actually, I've been expecting you for five minutes now, but I thought, 'nine o'clock sharp might be too eager?' She led me into the house.
[1086] Her teasing words gave me courage, even though I knew she didn't mean it. I was more direct than usual. "I am eager to get to the bottom of this and don't mind asking," I said, "Who wanted to kill Harriet Sipper, Ada Granceberry, and Richard Nix? I believe you have some ideas, and I want to know."
[1087] "What are those papers?"
[1088] "What? Oh!" I forgot I was still holding something, "It's a list, the list of guests and staff at the Blauxhan Hotel at the time of the incident. I thought you might want to look at it and tell me if you recognize anyone, but can you answer my question first? Who might have wanted to kill—"
[1089] "Nancy Drew," Margaret said. She took the two lists and read them carefully, frowning.
[1090] My reaction was the same as yesterday when Poirot faced Samuel Kid, only I didn't know it then. "Nancy Drew, the artist?"
[1091] "Wait a moment," Margaret said as she read the lists. "I'm afraid I don't recognize any of them."
[1092] "You mean Nancy Drew, the painter who paints portraits of society figures? The one who could have had a motive to kill Harriet Spear, Ada Gransbury, and Richard Nigg?"
[1093] Margaret folded the two papers and gave them back to me, then led me to the living room. We sat down in the chairs we had occupied yesterday, and she immediately spoke up. "Yes, it's Nancy Drew, the famous artist. I think she's the only one with a motive to kill Harriet Spear, Ada Gransbury, and Richard Nigg, and she has the ability to do it and get away with it. Mr. Carruthers, don't be surprised, celebrities can be evil too. Although I must admit, I don't believe Nancy would do such a thing. We met when she was very well-mannered, and I don't think she could change so quickly. She was a brave woman then."
[1094] I didn't say anything. I was thinking that the problem was that some killers had manners most of the time, but only needed to discard them once to commit murder.
[1095] Margaret said, "I didn't sleep last night, thinking it might be Walter Sturridge. No, it's not possible. He couldn't even stand up without help, let alone go to London. Killing three people is far beyond his abilities."
[1096] "Walter Stuart?" I leaned forward in my chair and said, "The old drunk I talked to at the King's Head last night? Why would he want to kill Harriet Sippey, Ada Gransbury, and Richard Nix?"
[1097] "Because Francis Ives is his daughter." Margaret turned back to the window and looked at the tombstones of the Ives couple. Suddenly, Shakespeare's lines from the Sonnets came to my mind: "Rumour's a dish that hath been long in the stew, and hath been dress'd and seasoned with old wives' tongues."
[1098] "I would be glad if Walter killed them," Margaret said, "am I terrible? As long as it's not Nancy, I'm fine. Walter is old, and I think he doesn't have much time left. Oh, I don't want it to be Nancy! I read in the newspaper how successful she was as an artist. She made a name for herself after leaving here, which is a comfort to me. Whenever I think of her succeeding in London, I feel happy."
[1099] "Leave here?" I said, "Did Nancy Drew used to live in Gleyholling too?"
[1100] Margaret stared out the window and said, "Yes, until 1913."
[1101] "The year Patric and Frances Ives died and Richard Nixes left the village."
[1102] "Yes."
[1103] "Margaret…" I leaned forward, trying to get her attention away from the Ives' graves. "I sincerely hope my arrival has convinced you to tell me the story of Patric and Frances Ives. I believe, after hearing their story, I will understand many of the current unresolved mysteries."
[1104] She turned to me, looking serious. "I've decided to tell you the story, but there's a condition. You must swear not to repeat it to anyone else in the village. Not a word of what I say in this room before you leave for London, and you can tell anyone you like after that."
[1105] "You needn't worry about that," I said. "I have few people to talk to in Gleeholm, and everyone runs away from me as soon as they see me. Even a child of no more than ten ran away from me this morning on my way to Margaret Enst's house. Even a child knows who I am and turns away to avoid me, knowing I'll try to get information out of them. Gleeholm has at least one advantage over London – they know how to ignore a person and make them feel uncomfortable."
[1106] "I hope you make a serious promise, Mr. Cuthbertson."
[1107] "Why the secrecy? Isn't everyone in the village aware of the Yew couple and what happened to them?"

[1109] "Why keep mentioning him if you want me to forget about him?"

[1111] "I'm afraid I cannot promise that. What am I supposed to report back to the Scotland Yard leaders? They sent me here to investigate the situation."

[1114] "No!" Her eyes filled with fear. "Please don't find Ambrose! You don't understand. You won't understand."

[1116] "Oh, I'll tell you everything, with no reservations."
