[1118] Margaret stood up and walked to the window, pressing her forehead against the glass, her entire body blocking my view of the Evanses' grave. "The incident that happened in 1913 left a painful wound on this village," she said calmly. "Everyone who lives here couldn't escape it. After that, Nancy Duane moved to London, Richard Nigg moved to Devonshire, but they couldn't escape either, carrying their wounds with them. They may not show it on their skin or bodies, but the wounds are there. The invisible wounds are the most painful. As for those who stayed, like Ambrose Flavard, they are also suffering. I don't know if Gleeholm can recover, but I know it hasn't yet."
[1119] She turned her head to look at me. "Mr. Kitcher, no one in the village has mentioned the tragedy since then. No one, not directly. Sometimes silence is the only way. Silence and forgetting, if only someone could forget."
[1120] "Are you worried that my questions might affect Dr. Flavard? Is he trying to forget the past?"
[1121] "As I said, forgetting is impossible."
[1122] "But... is it a painful topic for him?"
[1123] "Yes, it's very painful."
[1124] "Is he your friend?"
[1125] "I have nothing to do with this," she rebuked me sternly. "Ambrose is a good person, and I don't want him to be disturbed. Why won't you swear?"
[1126] "Okay, I swear." I reluctantly promised her, "I won't mention anything you said to anyone in the village."
[1127] The three portraits of the late Charles Ernest on the wall were staring at me, and his eyes seemed to be warning me: "You scoundrel, if you dare to deceive my wife, you will regret it."
[1128] "Is your heart at peace?" I asked her, "You don't want me to go to Dr. Flavard and talk about it because you're afraid he'll be sad. But I'm worried that my words may make you sad. I don't want you to be sad."
[1129] "Well." Margaret sighed, "In fact, I also want to take this opportunity to tell this story to someone like me."
[1130] "Then, please start." I said.
[1131] She nods and sits back in her chair, beginning to tell the story of Patrick and Francis Ives, without interruption from me. Here is the story as I write it down now:
[1132] All the trouble started sixteen years ago, from a rumor spread by a maid who worked for the Reverend Patrick Ives and his wife, Francis. Although the rumor started with her, she was not responsible for the later tragedies, nor did she bear the main responsibility. She spun a vicious lie, but only told one person, and had no part in the process of spreading it throughout the village. Indeed, after the unpleasant incident, she withdrew almost all of what she had said before, and no one saw her again. Some people speculate that she was ashamed of what she had done, so she hid herself away, and she should have been ashamed. Later, she regretted what she had done and tried to make amends, but it was too late.
[1133] Of course, telling one person and spreading such a lie was immoral. Perhaps it was because she had a difficult day, or perhaps, as a maid, she naturally resented the Ives couple. Perhaps she wanted to spice up her dull life with a little malicious lie and thought it wouldn't hurt anyone else seriously. Unfortunately, she chose Harriet Sipper as her only listener for her vile lie. She may have chosen her for a very simple reason: since her husband's death, Harriet had become bitter and resentful, and hearing such a lie would excite her and she would believe it, as she hoped it was true. There was someone in the village who had made a serious mistake, and even worse (in Harriet's eyes, this would be even better), it was the clergyman! She would certainly be delighted to hear the news and shine with excitement! Yes, Harriet was the best listener for the maid's vile lie, and she rightly chose her.
[1134] The maid told Harriet Sipper that Patrick Ives was a cruel liar who deserved to be damned. She said that while his wife Francis was out helping the parishioners, Patrick seduced the villagers late at night, claiming he could help them communicate with their deceased loved ones through spiritualism, claiming that deceased souls had entrusted him to convey their messages, and thus defrauding them of their money.
[1135] The end.
[1136] Harriet Sipper spread the rumor that Patrick had already swindled several villagers with this trick. She may have done this to escalate the situation and make the story more terrifying. Later, the maid insisted that she had only mentioned one name to Harriet, that of Nancy Duane.
[1137] At that time, Nancy was not yet a famous portrait painter, but an ordinary woman. In 1910, her husband William took up the position of headmaster at the Grellhollow village school, and she came with him. William was much older than Nancy, and when they married, Nancy was eighteen, and William was almost fifty. He died of respiratory disease in 1912.
[1138] In the malicious rumor spread by Harriet Sipper in the snowy January of 1913, Nancy had made several late-night or evening visits to the vicar's house, each time when it was already dark. Nancy always seemed shifty and chose the days when Frances Eve was not at home.
[1139] A sensible person would not believe such a story. In the dark, you can't tell if someone has a shifty expression on their face, and in fact, you can't see any expression at all. In the dark, you can't tell who the woman entering or leaving the vicar's house is, unless her gait is very distinctive, and Nancy Duane's gait was not particularly special. Of course, it is more likely that someone saw her several times and followed her home to find out who she was.
[1140] It is much easier to accept the views of someone more fanatical than to challenge them, and that is what the people of Grellhollow did. They willingly believed the rumor and began to condemn Patrick Eve along with Harriet, condemning him for profaning the gods and extorting money. Most people believed (or pretended to believe in order to avoid being slandered by Harriet) that Patrick Eve had deceived the parishioners by claiming to give messages to the living and the dead, charging exorbitant fees. In the eyes of the villagers of Grellhollow, Nancy Duane would not have been able to refuse such an arrangement, especially as it was provided by the parish vicar. Of course, she would have paid a considerable sum for such an arrangement.
[1141] The villagers forgot that they knew, liked, and trusted Patrick Ives. They ignored the fact that he was a decent and kind man, knowing that Harriet Cyples was fond of digging up dirt on bad people, but they did not care. They agreed with Harriet Cyples because they did not want to anger her, but this was not the only reason. The two allies of Harriet Cyples also played an important role: Richard Nix and Ada Granceberry supported her cause.
[1142] It is well known that Ada is the most devout woman in Gregholm. Her faith never wavered, and she always quoted from the New Testament when she spoke. She was admired and respected by everyone, but she was not a companion you would seek out when you had time to spare. She had once been engaged to the acknowledged young scholar Richard Nix, the lawyer.
[1143] Richard's exceptional intellect and noble demeanor earned him the respect of the entire village. He believed Harriet's lies because he had witnessed them firsthand. He had seen Nancy Duane, or a woman who looked like Nancy Duane, enter and leave the vicar's house on more than one occasion, and the vicar's wife was out visiting her father or attending to the needs of the parishioners.
[1144] Richard Nix believed the rumors, and Ada Granceberry believed them too. She could not bear the thought of a clergyman like Patrick engaging in such pagan behavior. She, Harriet, and Richard decided to remove Patrick Ives from his position as the parish vicar of Gregholm and expel him from the church. They wanted Patrick to confess his sins in public, but he refused to do so, believing the rumors to be unfounded.
[1145] The villagers' hatred of Patrick Ives soon spread to his wife, Francis, who they believed must have known about her husband's deception. Francis swore she did not know. At first, she tried to insist that Patrick would not do such a thing, but people kept saying it, and she eventually fell silent.
[1146] In Gleeholm, only two people refused to participate in the persecution of the Eve couple: Nancy Duane and Ambrose Flavord, the doctor who had publicly defended Francis Eve. He argued that if Francis knew such vile things were happening in her own home, why wasn't she present every time? Couldn't that prove her innocence? Ambrose Flavord also pointed out that it was impossible to discern guilt from someone's facial expression in the dark. He publicly stated that he would always believe his friend Patrick Eve, unless someone could provide undeniable evidence of the clergyman's wrongdoing. Ambrose Flavord also accused Harriet Sipper (in public, in front of everyone) of planting more dirt on Patrick Eve in half an hour than all the bad things he had done in his lifetime.
[1147] Despite this, Ambrose Flavord's viewpoint was not widely accepted, but he was one of the few who didn't care what others thought. He defended Patrick Eve in front of the church authorities, telling them that he believed the rumors to be baseless. He was also deeply concerned about Francis Eve's well-being, as she was in a terrible state. She stopped eating and barely slept, and would collapse whenever she had to leave the parsonage for even a moment. Patrick Eve was also mad, saying that his position as a clergyman and his reputation no longer mattered to him; all he wanted was for his wife to recover.
[1148] Meanwhile, Nancy Duane said nothing, neither confirming nor denying the rumors. The more Harriet provoked her, the more she seemed to remain silent. One day, Harriet suddenly changed her mind. She found Victor Mickle, telling him there was something important he needed to know that could stop this madness from continuing. After hearing her out, Victor Mickle chuckled to himself, rubbing his hands together, and slipped out the back door of the inn. Soon, everyone in Gleeholm knew that Nancy Duane had something to announce.
[1149] Besides the Eve couple, the entire village went to the King's Head Inn, including the maid who had first stirred up trouble, who had been missing for several weeks and now rushed over, eager to see how the drama would unfold.
[1150] Nancy Duane smiled at Ambrose Flavord before calmly telling everyone that Patrick Eve's claim of receiving payment for helping her communicate with her deceased husband was false, but she admitted that the rumors were not entirely untrue. She confessed that she had indeed visited the parsonage multiple times when the parson's wife was not present, as she had fallen in love with Patrick Eve.
[1151] The whole village was stunned. Some began to whisper, some covered their mouths with their hands, and others tightly grasped the arms of the people next to them.
[1152] After the commotion subsided, Nancy Duane continued: "Our secret meetings and mutual seduction were wrong, but we couldn't help it. Every time we met at his house, we just talked about our feelings for each other and about how we couldn't be together. We both thought we shouldn't meet again, but as soon as Francis went out... Oh, the power of love is irresistible."
[1153] Suddenly, someone shouted, "You only talked? What nonsense!" Nancy once again assured everyone that she and Patrick Yves had no physical contact.
[1154] "I have told you the truth," Nancy continued, "I didn't want to say the truth, but it's the only way to stop the stupid rumors from spreading. If someone knows what it's like to be irresistibly in love, they won't blame me and Patrick. And those who secretly condemn us in their hearts, you don't understand love, and I pity you."
[1155] Then, Nancy stared straight at Harriet Sipper and said, "Harriet, I believe you once understood the meaning of true love, but after losing George, you chose to forget everything. Even turned love into hate."
[1156] At this point, Harriet Sipper stood up, determined to prove herself innocent. She not only immediately condemned Nancy as a lying whore but also condemned Patrick Ivory even more madly: accusing him of not only making money through fraud in the name of spiritualism but also of being unfaithful to his wife by consorting with morally corrupt women. She called Patrick a pagan, an adulterer! More evil than she had ever imagined! She also said that a sinful person like him had no right to call himself a pastor of Gleghorn, which was a shame.
[1157] Nancy Duane couldn't bear Harriet's fierce attack and left the White Hart Inn in the middle. A few seconds later, the maid from the Ivory family also ran out of the inn, red-faced and crying.
[1158] Most of the villagers were at a loss and confused. At this point, Ada Gransby stood up to support Harriet. She said that although she didn't know which were facts and which were rumors, there was one thing that was certain: Patrick Ivory had to be guilty, so he couldn't be the pastor of Gleghorn anymore.
[1159] Yes, most of the villagers agreed. Yes, that's right.
[1160] At that time, Richard Nigges said nothing, even when his fiancée Ada pointed to him to say something, he didn't say anything. Later that day, he told Ambrose Flavard, the doctor, that the sudden turn of events worried him. He said, "The phrase 'a sinner' clearly pleased Ada, but he couldn't accept it himself. He also said that Harriet Sipper's accusation of Patrick Ivory's two sins together disgusted him. In the absence of evidence and without a chance to defend himself, she twisted Nancy Duane's "it's not like that, it's like that" into "it's like that, and it's like that."
[1161] Ada used the word "without a doubt" at the King's Head Inn. Richard Nixes told Ambrose Flavard that now, in his opinion, it was without a doubt that people (including himself, which made him feel ashamed) were slandering Patrick Ives. What if Nancy Duane was lying? What if her love was just unrequited, and Patrick Ives agreed to meet her secretly just to explain that she should keep her feelings to herself?
[1162] Dr. Flavard also agreed: no one could be sure whether Patrick Ives had done something wrong or not, and it was from the beginning that others had arbitrarily defined it. As the only one who was willing to let him into their house, the doctor went to visit him again and told Patrick everything Nancy Duane had said at the King's Head Inn. Patrick only shook his head after hearing it, and did not make any comments on whether what Nancy said was true or false. As for Frances Ives, both physically and mentally, she was getting worse and worse.
[1163] Richard Nixes failed to convince Ada Gransbury to accept his point of view, so their relationship became tense. Under Harriet's leadership, the villagers continued to condemn the Ives couple, shouting at them day and night outside the vicarage. At the same time, Ada also petitioned the church to expel the Ives couple from the vicarage and the Glynhill parish.
[1164] Tragedy then occurred. Frances Ives could no longer bear these insults and committed suicide by poisoning, ending her miserable life. When Patrick found her, he knew it was too late, and there was no need to call Dr. Flavard. Frances could not be saved. Patrick knew that he could no longer survive in the guilt and suffering, so he also ended his life.
[1165] Ada Gransbury suggested that the villagers pray for the souls of Patrick and Frances Ives, asking God to forgive them, although it was unlikely that God would forgive them.
[1166] Harriet Sipper believed that there was no need for God to judge this matter, and that the Eves couple would be forever tormented by the fires of hell. She told the "executors of justice" who followed her that the Eves couple deserved it.
[1167] After the death of the Eves couple, Richard Niggles broke off his engagement with Edith Gransbury and left Gleholling within a few months. Nancy Duane went to London, and the maid who had spun the terrible lies disappeared without a trace, and the villagers never saw her again.
[1168] At the same time, Charles and Margaret Ernst came to the village and took over the parish. They quickly became good friends with Dr. Ambrose Flavard, who voluntarily told them the tragic story. He also told them that, whether Patrick Eves really had feelings for Nancy Duane or not, he was the most tolerant and kind-hearted person he had ever seen, and should not have been slandered.
[1169] It was because he mentioned the slander that Margaret Ernst wanted to engrave the poem on the tombstone. Charles Ernst opposed this idea, not wanting to anger the villagers, but Margaret insisted, believing that the Church of the Saints should show support for Patrick and Francis Eves. Margaret said, "I don't just want to anger Harriet Sipper and Edith Gransbury." Yes, when she said this, she had already thought of murder, although only in thought, not with the intention of committing a crime.
[1170] After the story was told, Margaret Ernst fell silent. For a while, neither of us spoke.
[1171] It was I who finally spoke: "Now I understand why, when I asked you who might have a motive for murder, you mentioned Nancy Duane. But would she have killed Richard Nigges? He suspected the maid of lying and stopped supporting Harriet Sipper and Ada Gransberry immediately."
[1172] "I can only tell you how I would think if I were Nancy," Margaret said. "Would I forgive Richard Nigges? No, I wouldn't. It was his initial support for Harriet and the evil maid's lies that caused Ada Gransberry to believe those absurdities. The three of them, Harriet Sipper, Ada Gransberry, and Richard Nigges, conspired to incite the villagers against Patrick Yves."
[1173] "What about the maid?"
[1174] "Ambrose Flavard believes she didn't know things would turn out this way. She was clearly not happy when everyone in the village began to dislike the Yves couple."
[1175] I frowned, unsatisfied. "But for a Nancy with a murderous intent - I just want to discuss this - even though Richard Nigges later realized he was wrong, she still couldn't forgive him. How could she forgive the maid who first spread the lies?"
[1176] "Maybe she didn't forgive the maid either," Margaret said. "Maybe she killed her too. I don't know where the maid went, but maybe Nancy knows. Nancy might have found her and killed her. Your face is very pale. What's wrong?"
[1177] "The… the maid who spread the rumors was… what was her name?" I stuttered, worried that I already knew her name, "No, no, it can't be. But my mind is saying, 'How could it not be?'"
[1178] "Jenny Hobbs. Mr. Cutter, are you alright? You don't look well."
[1179] "He's right! She's in danger."
[1180] "Who's 'he'?"
[1181] "Hercule Poirot. He's always right. How could this be possible?"
[1182] "Why do you sound so unhappy? Do you wish he was wrong?"
[1183] "No. I think he's right. I'm just worried about Jenny Hobbs' safety now, if she's still alive."
[1184] "I see. That's strange."
[1185] "What's strange?"
[1186] Margaret sighed. "I've said everything, but I still don't think Nancy would threaten anyone. Whether she had a motive or not, I don't think she would kill. It sounds strange, but… the person who kills is also terrified and depressed by it—don't you think?"
[1187] I nodded.
[1188] "Nancy enjoys an interesting, beautiful, happy, and loving life. She loves everything good. She would never get involved in such an ugly thing as murder."
[1189] "So, if not Nancy Duane, who then?" I asked. "Walter Sturridge, the drunken old man who is Francis Yew's father? He had a strong motive to kill. If he could go a day or two without drinking, he would have the ability to kill three people."
[1190] "It's impossible for Walter to go an hour without drinking. I can assure you, Mr. Cattermole, that Walter Sturridge is not the person you're looking for. You see, unlike Nancy Duane, he never blamed Harry, Ada, and Richard for what happened to Francis. He only blamed himself."
[1191] "So that's why he's drinking?"
[1192] "Yes. After losing his daughter, Walter Sturridge most wanted to kill himself. I think he'll succeed soon."
[1193] "Why does he think it's his fault that Francis committed suicide?"
[1194] "Walter didn't live in Grellingholme before. He moved here to be closer to the resting place of Patrick and Francis. You might find it hard to believe how he was before Francis died. Walter Sturridge was a distinguished classical scholar and a teacher at Jesus College, Cambridge University. Patrick Yve studied to become a priest at that college. Patrick had no parents and became an orphan at a young age, and Walter could be considered his guardian. At that time, Jenny Hobbs was seventeen years old and worked as a dormitory supervisor at the university. She was the best and most devout dormitory supervisor, so Walter Sturridge arranged for her to take care of Patrick's room. Later, Patrick and Walter's daughter, Francis Sturridge, got married. When they moved to St. Michael's Church in Grellingholme, Jenny came along too. Do you understand?"
[1195] I nodded. Margaret continued, "Walter Sturridge blames himself for bringing Jenny Hobbs and Patrick Yve here. If Patrick and Francis hadn't brought Jenny to Grellingholme, she wouldn't have had the chance to spread that terrible lie, leading them to commit suicide. I wouldn't have to spend my life guarding their graves, preventing them from being disturbed."
[1196] "Who would do such a thing?" I asked, "Harriet Sipper? I mean, before she was killed."
[1197] "Oh, no. Harriet's weapon is her sharp tongue, not her hands. She would never destroy a gravestone. It was not her, but the rough young people in the village who did it. They had heard the stories from their parents and took advantage of any opportunity to act on them. Patrick and Frances were still children when they died, but they heard the tales. If you ask the people here, except for me and Ambrose Flavard, they will all say that Patrick Yve was an evil man and that he and his wife practiced witchcraft. As time goes by, I believe more and more people come to believe this is the truth. They have to believe, don't they? They either believe or they despise themselves from the bottom of their hearts like I do."
[1198] I have one more thing to clarify. "Did Richard Neagles sever ties with Ada Gransbury because he regained his sanity, but Ada continued to defame Patrick Yves? Did he call off the engagement after hearing Nancy's frank words at the Royal Hotel?"
[1199] Margaret's face flickered with a strange expression. She said, "What happened at the King's Head was just the beginning…" She suddenly stopped and switched to another topic, "Yes, he found Ada and Harriet's unreasonable insistence to be somewhat annoying and unbearable."
[1200] Suddenly, Margaret's face showed a look of significance. I felt certain that there was something important she had decided not to tell me.
[1201] "You said that Frances Yew committed suicide by poison," I said. "How exactly? Where did she get the poison? And how did Patrick Yew die?"
[1202] "The same way: by poison. I don't think you've heard of the poison from love beans, have you?"
[1203] "No, I haven't."
[1204] "It's a plant called chicken pearl, very common in tropical regions. Frances Yew somehow got hold of a few small bottles of this stuff."
[1205] "I'm sorry, but since they both took the same poison and died together, how can you be sure that Frances committed suicide first, and Patrick followed her after discovering she was dead?"
[1206] Margaret looked cautious and said, "You won't tell the villagers of Gleeham what I've told you, will you? Just tell the people at Scotland Yard?"
[1207] "Yes."
[1208] "Francis Yves left a suicide note for her husband, " Margaret said, "It simply stated that she wanted him to live well. Patrick also left a message…" She suddenly stopped.
[1209] I waited.
[1210] Finally, she said, "We can determine the sequence of events from the two notes."
[1211] "Where are the notes?"
[1212] "I destroyed them. Ambrose Flavard gave them to me, and I threw them into the fire."
[1213] This puzzled me. "Why did you do that?" I asked her.
[1214] "I…" Margaret sniffed and turned away, firmly saying, "I don't know."
[1215] I pondered to myself, she must know. Her tightly closed lips told me she didn't want to say any more about it. If I kept pushing, it might only make her more determined.
[1216] I stood up and stretched my cramped legs. "You're right about one thing," I said. "Now that I know the story of Patrick and Francis Yves, I do want to talk to Ambrose Flavard, the doctor. He was living in the village at the time. Whatever you say—"
[1217] "No. You swore to me."
[1218] "I want to ask him some things, like about Jenny Hobbs."
[1219] "I can tell you about Jenny. What do you want to know? Patrick and Francis Yves seemed to need her very much. They liked her a lot. Before she lied, everyone in the village thought Jenny was quiet, polite, and harmless. Personally, I don't believe someone who can make up stories out of thin air is harmless. She was too ambitious, even her tone of voice changed."
[1220] "How did it change?"
[1221] "Ambrose said it changed suddenly. One day, she spoke like a housekeeper, and the next day, her words were different, but very appropriate."
[1222] I thought to myself, grammar-wise, she was accurate. Oh, don't let them open their mouths - three mouths, each with a cufflink engraved with initials: grammar was accurate. In that sense, Poirot was right.
[1223] "Ambrose said Jeanne was imitating the tone of Patrick and Frances Ives. They were both well-educated and spoke very politely."
[1224] "Margaret, tell me the truth. Why won't you let me talk to Ambrose Flavard? Are you afraid he'll tell me something you don't want me to know?"
[1225] "Going to Ambrose won't help you, it will only bother him. I've already allowed you to scare the other villagers. They're already scared enough. The guilt is contagious, and deep down, they all know they're guilty. But if they hear what you, as a police officer, have to say, that the killer will only stop when he puts all the people who helped persecute Patrick and Frances Ives into hell, they will be even more scared."
[1226] "That's too extreme."
[1227] "I do have a peculiar sense of humor, Charles has scolded me for it before. I never told him that I don't believe in heaven and hell. Oh, I believe in God, but not the one we usually talk about."
[1228] "I must have looked very tense at the time. I didn't want to discuss theology, I just wanted to get back to London and tell Pollock what I had seen and heard."
[1229] "Of course, there is only one God, but I don't believe he only wants us to follow principles and not question, or to be rude to those who violate principles." She smiled again, this time more mildly, "I think God's way of doing things is similar to mine, but different from Ada Gransebury's. Do you agree?"
[1230] I grumbled in response.
[1231] "The Church teaches that only God has the power to judge," Margaret said. "Why didn't the devout Ada Gransebury point this out to Harriet Sippel and the other followers? Why did she lay all the blame on Patrick Eves? If someone wants to claim to be a devout Christian, they should first follow the basic tenets."
[1232] "It's clear that you're still upset about this."
[1233] "I'll never stop being angry, Mr. Cattermole. It's ugly for someone to accuse someone who's only made a small mistake, under the guise of morality, of being the most sinful. Isn't that worth being angry about?"
[1234] "Hypocrisy is ugly," I agreed with her view.
[1235] "Also, some people say that for true love, nothing is wrong."
[1236] "I'm not entirely sure about that," I replied. "If someone is married—"
[1237] "Oh, what nonsense about marriage!" Margaret said, looking up at the three portraits on the wall. "I'm sorry, dear Charles, but if two people truly love each other, even if it causes trouble for the church or goes against doctrine... love is love, isn't it? I know you don't like me saying that."
[1238] I didn't particularly like it either. "Love brings a lot of trouble," I said. "If Nancy Dunn hadn't fallen in love with Patrick Ivory, I wouldn't be investigating three murders here."
[1239] "Nonsense!" Margaret scoffed. "It was hate that killed, Mr. Kitcher, not love. It will never be love. Please, keep your wits about you."
[1240] "I've always believed that the harshest rules test a person's character the most," I said to her.
[1241] "That's true. But what quality are we testing? Do we trust others too easily? Maybe. Idiotic behavior. The Bible, full of rules and teachings, is just a book written by one or a few people. It should have a disclaimer at the front: 'God's will may be distorted or misinterpreted.'"
[1242] "I must go. I'm heading back to London. Thank you for your help, it means a lot to us."

[1244] "That's very kind of you to say."
[1245] "I've lived my life by the teachings of that dusty old book, Mr. Caster, so I know how foolish it is. But that doesn't mean I can tolerate murder. I won't. Alright, you can go now, or I'll keep talking."
[1246] On my way back to the Wigmore Hotel, I pondered how strange it is to be taken on a journey by conversation, only to be left stranded at a thousand miles from the starting point with no way back. Margaret Ernst's words echoed in my ears: "Even against the teachings... but love is love, isn't it?"
[1247] Entering the Wigmore Hotel, I strode past the snoring Walter Sturridge and the defiantly staring Victor McKeen, straight up to my room to pack.
[1248] I caught the next train to London, glad to be leaving the village, but still wanting to talk to Dr. Ambrose Flavard. If I told Poirot that I'd sworn to Margaret Ernst, what would he say? He wouldn't approve, and he'd scold me for my Britishness and our poor sense of humor. I'd have to hang my head and mutter apologies, unable to reveal my true thoughts. However, I believed that by respecting the oath I'd sworn, I would gain more information than expected. By leaving the impression that you won't force people to speak, they often approach you when they're ready to reveal the answers you seek.
[1249] I knew Poirot wouldn't agree with my viewpoint, he doesn't care about these things. But since Margaret Ernst doesn't even agree with God, it's okay for me to occasionally disagree with the great Hercule Poirot. If he wants to investigate Dr. Ambrose Flavard, he can go to Greyleave and talk to him himself.
[1250] However, I hope it won't be necessary. Nancy Drew is the one we should focus on. And ensuring the safety of Jenny - if she's still alive. I regret not considering the possibility of Jenny being in danger. If we truly saved her, the credit would go to Poirot. If we successfully solve the triple murder at the Brawley Hotel, the credit would again be Poirot's. Officially, within Scotland Yard, the credit might be recorded under my name, but everyone knows it's Poirot's, not mine. Indeed, and thanks to my superiors knowing about Poirot's involvement, they let me proceed as I saw fit - perhaps I should say as my Belgian friend saw fit. They trust Poirot, not me.
[1251] Suddenly, I wondered if I would still enjoy this independent investigation if the credit were to go entirely to Poirot, but I didn't have an answer before I fell asleep.
[1252] I had a dream, my first dream on a train. In the dream, everyone was condemning me for something I hadn't done. In the dream, I clearly saw my own gravestone, with my name replacing that of Patrick and Frances Ives, and the famous "Rumor Has It" sonnet engraved below. On the ground beneath the gravestone, there was a gleaming piece of metal that I knew was a cufflink with my initials engraved on it, half buried in the earth and half exposed. I woke up, drenched in sweat, my heart pounding in my chest as the train pulled into London station.
[1253] Chapter Thirteen
[1254] Nancy Drew
[1255] Of course, I didn't know that Poirot already knew that Nancy Drew might be involved in the triple murder. While I was on the train back to London from Grellanhole, Poirot was busy planning with the help of Scotland Yard to visit the Drew woman's home in London.
[1256] According to the plan, that afternoon, he went with Inspector Stanley. Their house was located in Belgravia [1], a large white villa with a young maid in an apron opening the door. Poirot expected to be taken to a tastefully decorated living room to wait, so he was surprised to see Nancy Drew standing at the foot of the staircase.
[1257] "Mr. Poirot, welcome. I see you've brought a policeman with you, and I imagine that means something quite serious is going on."
[1258] Stanley Bill's throat made a strange sound, and his face turned red. Nancy Drew was very beautiful, with a face as pink as a peach, black and shiny hair, and deep blue eyes under long lashes. She looked to be around forty years old, wearing a fashionable dress in shades of peacock blue and deep green. For the first time, Poirot was not the most elegantly dressed person in the room.
[1259] "Mrs. Drew, it's a pleasure to meet you. I admire your artistic talent, and I've had the honor of seeing a few of your works at exhibitions recently. You have a truly precious gift."
[1260] "Thank you. You flatter me. Now, let's take off your coat and hat, and we'll find a comfortable place to sit and chat. Would you like some tea or coffee?"
[1261] "Yes, thank you."
[1262] "Great. Please follow me."
[1263] They went to a small living room, and I'm glad I only have to listen to the description later, as Poirot described the room to be full of portraits. The walls were covered with watchful eyes...
[1264] Poirot asked Miss Nancy Duane if all the portraits on the walls were her paintings.
[1265] "Oh, no," she said, "not many of them are mine. I buy as many paintings as I sell, and I think it's better. I love art."
[1266] "So do I," Poirot told her.
[1267] "It can be lonely to only appreciate your own work, and hard to bear. I often think of hanging another artist's work on the wall as hanging a good friend."
[1268] "I agree. You explained it very well, Madame."
[1269] They had just sat down when Nancy said, "Allow me to get straight to the point. What brings you here? You said on the phone that you wanted to search my home. You are certainly welcome to do so. But why?"
[1270] "Madame, you may have seen in the papers that three guests at the Browns Hotel were killed last Thursday evening."
[1271] "At the Bluxton Hotel? " Nancy smiled, then her face fell. "Oh, dear, is that true? Three? You're sure? I always thought Bluxton was a high-class hotel, I can't imagine there would be murders there."
[1272] "So you know the hotel?"
[1273] "Yes, I often go there for afternoon tea. Their manager, Lazari, is very likable. You know, their scones are famous, the best in London. Sorry... " She paused, "Three people were killed there, I shouldn't be talking about scones here. It's terrible. But I don't see how it's related to me."
[1274] "You didn't see the newspaper report?" Polo asked.
[1275] "No." Nancy Dunn's lips were in a straight line. "I don't read newspapers, and we don't subscribe to them. The newspapers are full of misfortunes. I don't want to see misfortunes."
[1276] "So you don't know the names of the three victims?"
[1277] "No, I don't want to know."
[1278] "Well, whether you want to or not, I'm afraid I have to tell you. Their names are Harriet Sipper, Edith Gransbury, and Richard Nigg."
[1279] "Oh, no, no. Oh, Mr. Poirot! I'm not joking, am I? Tell me what's going on."
[1280] "This is not a joke, madam. I'm sorry to upset you."
[1281] "Hearing their names does upset me. Whether they're alive or dead, it doesn't matter to me. I don't intend to remember them. You see, the more you try to forget something, the more it sticks with you, and… I hate seeing misery more than most people."
[1282] "Have you experienced a lot of misery?"
[1283] "I don't want to talk about personal matters."
[1284] Poirot was in a difficult position, as he wanted to talk about everything she didn't want to talk about. But there was nothing he liked more than the personal matters of strangers he might never see again.
[1285] He changed the subject and asked, "Let's talk about the purpose of my visit, something related to the police investigation. Do you know the three victims?"
[1286] Nancy nodded. "I used to live in a village called Gleeholm in Pigeon Valley. You wouldn't know the place, no one does. Harry, Ada, and Richard were my neighbors until I moved to London in 1913. I haven't seen or heard from them since. Were they killed?"
[1287] "Yes, ma'am."
[1288] "At the Blauxhams Hotel? What were they doing there? Why did they come to London?"
[1289] "That's one of the questions I wanted to ask," Poirot said to her.
[1290] "It makes no sense to kill them," Nancy said, standing up from her chair and walking back and forth between the door and the opposite wall. "The only person who would do such a thing didn't do it!"
[1291] "Who is that person?"
[1292] "Oh, don't look at me," Nancy sat back down in her chair, "Sorry. This news has shocked me. I can't help you. You see... I don't want to be impolite, but I would like you to leave now."
[1293] "Madam, you mean that only you could have killed these three people? But you didn't kill them, did you?"
[1294] "I didn't...," Nancy looked around the room, slowly saying, "Ah, now I understand why you came here. You heard some stories and thought I killed them, so you wanted to search my house. But I didn't kill anyone. However, to satisfy your curiosity, Monsieur Poirot, let Tabitha show you around every room. My house has many rooms, and you might miss a few if she doesn't show you."
[1295] "Thank you, madam."
[1296] "You won't find any evidence of a crime because there isn't any. I hope you leave soon! You have no idea how much I'm hurt."
[1297] Stanley Bill stood up. "Well, I'll begin. Thank you for your cooperation, Madam." He left the drawing room and closed the door.
[1298] "You are so clever, aren't you, Madam Dunn?" Nancy said to Poirot, as if protesting. "You are as clever as people say. I can see it in your eyes."
[1299] "People say I have an extraordinary brain. Yes, I do."
[1300] "You are so proud. In my opinion, an extraordinary brain is useless unless it is accompanied by an extraordinary heart."
[1301] "Of course, as a lover of great art, we must believe this. The heart and soul can perceive art better than the brain."
[1302] "I agree." Nancy said quietly, "You know, Mr. Poirot, your eyes...they're not just clever. They're wise. They can connect to the distant past. Oh, you probably don't understand what I mean, but that's the truth. They must be beautiful in a painting, but I won't give you a portrait, especially with those three terrible names on my doorstep today."
[1303] "That's unfortunate."
[1304] "It's your fault." Nancy folded her hands, speaking honestly, "Oh, I think I should tell you: what I just said was about myself. If someone wanted to kill Harriet, Ada, and Richard, it would be me, but I also said I didn't do it. So, I have no idea what happened."
[1305] "You don't like them?"
[1306] "I hate them. I've wished them dead countless times. Oh, dear!"

I finished her portrait and delivered it, then stayed for dinner. I think I arrived at around 6:00 p.m. and stayed until about 10:00 p.m. I might have stayed longer if it weren't for her husband, Saint John. He's such a snob, and she's so sweet, never nitpicking about anyone's faults, you know the type. She insisted that Saint John and I would get along well, being artists, but I can't stand him. He claims his art is superior to mine and never misses an opportunity to show it off. He's obsessed with painting plants and fish, boring leaves or cold-eyed cod and haddock!"
[1313] "Is he an animal or plant artist?"

[1456] Chapter Fourteen
[1457] Thoughts in the Mirror
[1458] As soon as I arrived in London, I went to "The Happy Coffee House" hoping to see Poirot. It turned out that only one person I knew was there, the waitress "Pompadour Head" mentioned by Poirot. She seemed to be like an elixir that made everything in "The Happy Coffee House" more enjoyable. What was her name again? Poirot had told me. Oh, right, her name was Phyllis Spurling, with Phyllis being a nickname for Euphemia.
[1459] I liked her because she always repeated two things to me whenever she saw me: her lifelong wish to change "The Happy Coffee House" into "The Happy Tea Room," reflecting her preference for the two beverages; and, "How did Scotland Yard treat you? I'd love to work there too—but only if I'm in charge, otherwise, no deal."
[1460] "Oh, I'm sure you'll make it to the top soon," I told her. "Just like I'm sure that one day I'll come here and find the sign outside reads 'The Happy Tea Room.'"
[1461] "Impossible. They won't let me change that," she said. "Poirot wouldn't like it, would he?"
[1462] "He'd be horrified."
[1463] "Don't tell him, or anyone else," Phyllida wanted to change the name of the place she worked, but she only told me about it, never anyone else.
[1464] "I won't say anything," I assured her. "Listen, come help me solve the case, and I'll suggest to our leadership to change our unit's name to 'Scotland Yard Tea Room'. We do drink tea there, so it's not inappropriate, right?"
[1465] "Pah," Phyllida was not convinced. "I heard that married women have to quit the police force. Well, I'd rather solve cases with you than stay home and look after a man."
[1466] "Then come on!"
[1467] "But don't ask me to marry you."
[1468] "Don't worry!"
[1469] "I'm very attractive, don't you think?"
[1470] To avoid getting into trouble, I replied, "I won't ask anyone to marry me, but if my parents point a gun at my head and force me, I'll come to you first. How about that?"
[1471] "Better to ask me than those girls with their heads full of romance. They're always disappointed, that's for sure."
[1472] I didn't want to talk about romance, so I said, "Speaking of working together on a case... aren't you waiting for Poirot? I thought he'd be here waiting for Jenny Hobbs."
[1473] "She's Jenny Hobbs? So you know her last name. Poirot would be delighted to know that, he's been so worried about it these days. He probably wouldn't bother me anymore if he knew. Every time I turn around, he's asking me about Jenny, and I've answered those questions so many times."
[1474] Her last statement made me feel very embarrassed, so I said, "Why?"
[1475] "I just won't ask. Make sure you think about what you're asking before you ask. What did you find out about Jenny?"
[1476] "I'm afraid I can't tell you."
[1477] "I'll tell you something in return. Mr. Poirot would want to know. Last Thursday, the night Miss Havisham came, she was always here when she was in a state of confusion. I told Mr. Poirot what I noticed at the time, but I forgot. But now I remember. It was a dark night, and I didn't pull the curtains. I never pull the curtains. I often think that the light from the coffee shop can illuminate the entire alley, and if someone sees the light, they might come in to sit. Especially if they see you through the window."
[1478] "Especially if they see you through the window." I flattered her.
[1479] She widened her eyes and said, "That's it."
[1480] "What does that mean?"
[1481] "After I asked Jenny to close the door, she rushed to the window and stared outside, as if someone was chasing her. She just stared at the window, staring, but she could only see herself, this room, and me - I mean my reflection. And I saw her and recognized her. You can ask Poirot, he knows this part too. Before she turned around, I said, 'Oh, it's you.' You see, if the café is lit up and it's pitch black outside, the glass of the window will act like a mirror. You might think she was trying to see what was outside, but it wasn't like that.”
[1482] "What does that mean?"
[1483] "She wasn't looking outside to see if someone was chasing her, she was looking at me, just like I was looking at her. I could see her reflection, and she could see mine, like looking in a mirror. Can you understand that?"
[1484] I nodded and said, "As long as you can see someone in the mirror, they can see you too."
[1485] "Absolutely correct. And Jenny was looking at me then, I swear, she was waiting to see what I would say or do, like observing. Mr. Cattermole, this may sound ridiculous, but at that time, I felt like I could see more than her. I could tell what she was thinking, and it sounded like a crazy idea. But I swear, she was waiting for me to serve her.”
[1486] "Any rational person who comes here would wait for you to serve them." I smiled.
[1487] "Pfft." Phyllida made a disapproving noise, "I don't know why I forgot about this. I swear, I didn't make it up... The girl in the glass was staring at my eyes, as if...," Phyllida furrowed her brow, "as if I was the dangerous one, not someone on the street outside. But why was she looking at me like that? Can you understand that? I can't."
[1488] I went to Scotland Yard first, and when I got back to the apartment, I met Poirot at the door, preparing to leave. He stood by the door, holding his coat and hat, with a flushed face and an anxious look, as if he couldn't keep his composure. Normally, nothing could bother him. Mrs. Bunchworth was also acting strange, ignoring me and complaining that the car hadn't arrived yet. Her face was also flushed.
[1489] "We must go to the Bluxton Hotel now, Mr. Cattermole. As soon as the car arrives, we'll leave," Poirot said, running his gloved fingers through his mustache.
[1490] "The car should have been here ten minutes ago," Mrs. Bunchworth said. "Although it's late, you can take Mr. Cattermole with you."
[1491] "Is there an emergency?" I asked him.
[1492] "Another murder," Poirot said. "At the Brawley's Hotel."
[1493] "Oh, dear," I felt a momentary rush of shame and panic coursing through my veins. It had happened: a neat row of bodies. One, two, three, four...
[1494] Eight lifeless hands, palm down...
[1495] Hold his hand, Edward...
[1496] "Is it Jean Hobby?" I asked Poirot, as if I could hear blood rushing in my ears.
[1497] I should have taken his warnings more seriously. How could I have been so careless?
[1498] "I don't know. Ah! So you know her name too. Mr. Lazarus called to inform us. The car is finally here."

[1500] "Of course, I will."
[1501] She wore an expression of great pain and said, "If I were you, I wouldn't go there. That man, the one who was just killed, what was he doing there? [1502] I should repeat these words verbatim to the corpse, I thought to myself. It might make me feel better if I could say them with a smile.

[1508] "What?"

[1510] He looked down on me and said, "If you think that, you're the favorite of the murderers. It's clear I failed!" [1511]I told him the whole trip, and as I spoke, I felt myself slowly coming back to normal. [1513]"Why?"
[1514]He didn't answer my question but asked another one.
[1515]"Did you secretly follow Margaret Ernst after your first visit to her house?"
[1516] "Follow her? No. I felt she would not go anywhere. She seemed to be staring out the window at the grave of the Yve couple all the time."

[1518] "No, I never thought of that. She seemed like a woman who would carefully consider things before making a major decision and not one who would seek advice from friends. So, I didn't suspect anything."
[1519] "But I do suspect," said Poirot. "I suspect Margaret Ernst wanted to consult with Dr. Ambrose Flavard about what she should say."
[1520] "Well, if she did consult someone, it would only be him," I admitted. "She did mention his name several times during our conversation. It was clear she admired him."
[1521] "But you didn't investigate Dr. Ambrose," Poirot said, a little angry. "You were too loyal to your oath. And also, it's the British politeness that makes you say 'worship' instead of 'love.' [1522] I grumbled in response, not caring. In my opinion, Margaret Ernst was a woman who only cared about rules and was crazy about the injustice that happened to the Evans couple, but I knew it was stupid to say it out loud. [1525] "Ah! This mystery is already solved."
[1526] "Solved? Should I say that this puzzle has been created, right?" [1]
[1527] "Oh, Ketteridge, if I tell you the answer, because I pity you, I won't let you exercise your independent thinking ability - but you must think! [1532] Chapter 15
[1533] The Fourth Pin

If someone commits a crime, they must face some form of legal sanction, without personal bias or a desire for revenge. The desire for revenge taints everything, and that's true evil."
[1674] "The desire for revenge." Nancy Dunn shuddered as she repeated the words, "That's it. Harriet Sipper was filled with a desire for revenge, and it was disgusting."
[1675] "But you didn't condemn Harriet Sipper in anger in the story," I said, "You only said her actions were disgusting and made you very sad. But it seems you're angrier at Jennie Hobbs than her."
[1676] "I think so," sighed Nancy. "I used to like Harriet. When my husband William and I first moved to Glebeholme, Harriet and George Sippel were our best friends. After George's death, Harriet turned into a monster. It's hard to condemn someone you once liked very much, didn't you notice?"
[1677] "I couldn't," said Poirot.
[1678] "I can't. I would see their bad behavior as a manifestation of a pathology, not the real them. But I cannot forgive Harriet for what she did to Patrick, and I cannot convince myself to forgive her. And I think the one who suffers the most from this transformation is herself."
[1679] "Do you consider her a victim?"
[1680] "Yes, she's young and has lost her beloved husband, and she's very unlucky! I believe one can be both a victim and a perpetrator."
[1681] "You and Harriet have something in common," Poirot said. "You both lost your husbands at a young age."
[1682] "It sounds callous, but we can't compare ourselves," Nancy said. "George Sipper was Harriet's whole life. I married William because he was intelligent, gave me security, and I needed to get away from home, away from my father."
[1683] "Oh, yes. Alcun Johnson." Poirot said. "I remembered who he was after leaving your house last time. At the end of the last century, your father was a notorious Anglo-Russian radical and spent some time in prison."
[1684] "He's a dangerous man." Nancy said. "I don't want to talk about his... views, but I must say. How can they make the world better?"
[1685] "I completely agree with your views, madam. A movement fueled by anger and hatred will not make our lives better. It's impossible because the source is wrong!"
[1686] I almost agreed, but I didn't say it. No one would be interested in my thoughts.
[1687] "When I met William Duane," said Nancy, "I didn't fall in love with him, I just liked him and respected him. He was calm, humble, and spoke or acted with decorum. For example, if he didn't return a book to the library by the due date, he would be very upset."
[1688] "A man with a conscience."
[1689] "Yes, he was humble and measured. If something stood in his way, he first thought of going around it, rather than moving it. [1711] Chapter 17

[1750] Chapter Eighteen
[1751] Knock, knock, see who answers
[1752] The next morning, we made our way through the city, preparing to unravel the mystery of our "visit." Poirot's mood was as changeable as the London weather, one moment cheerful and carefree, the next frowning and worried.
[1753] Finally, we arrived on a narrow street, standing in front of a modest little house. "Number 3, Jermyn Street," said Poirot outside. "Kid, do you know who we are going to see now? Familiar with the place?"

Whatever wrong you did in the tragedy involving Patrick and Frances Ives, the three people who should bear the same or at least equal blame are Harriet Sipper, Ada Gransbury, and Richard Nigg. They believed your lies and accused the Ives of their own accord. Moreover, at the 'Joyful Coffee House,' you told me, 'Only when I am dead will justice be served.' You emphasized 'I' and said 'only when I am dead.' This made me think that you already knew that Harriet Sipper, Ada Gransbury, and Richard Nigg had been killed. But now, with the evidence at hand, I believe that these three murders had not yet occurred at that time."
[1829] "Please, stop! Stop!" cried Jane, sobbing.
[1830] "Please wait a moment longer. When you told me, 'Only when I am dead will justice be served,' it was approximately 7:45, but now we know that the employees at the Browns Hotel discovered the three bodies at 8:10. So, Jane Hobbs, how did you know about these murders beforehand?"
[1831] "If you can stop, I will tell you everything! I've been keeping secrets and lying, which has been a torture for me. I can't take it anymore!"
[1832] "Okay." Poirot said calmly, his tone suddenly friendly, "Does this scare you? Have you realized that you can't fool Poirot?"
[1833] "I understand. Let me tell you the story from beginning to end. Finally, I can say it, finally I can breathe a sigh of relief."
[1834] Jenny began to tell the story. Hercule Poirot and I did not interrupt her until she finished. Here is her story, which I hope to faithfully and completely convey.

[1837] Chapter Nineteen
[1838] The Truth Revealed
[1839] I ruined the life of the only man I ever loved, and destroyed my own.
[1840] I didn't mean for things to turn out this way. I never imagined that the cruel words I uttered would cause such devastation. I should have thought twice, held my tongue, but I was hurt and vulnerable, and my anger got the better of me.
[1841] I loved Patrick Ivie wholeheartedly. I tried not to, but as soon as I started serving him, making his bed at Jesus College, Cambridge, where he was studying, I was engaged to Sam Kidd. I liked Sam a lot, but after meeting Patrick, my heart belonged to him, and I knew that no matter how hard I tried, this feeling would not change. Patrick had everything a man should have. He also liked me, but to him, I was just a servant. Even after I started speaking like the daughter of a Cambridge teacher, like Frances Ivie, in Patrick's eyes, I was still just a loyal servant, nothing more.
[1842] Of course, I knew about his affair with Nancy Dunn. I overheard their conversations, but not intentionally. Knowing how much he loved her made it unbearable for me. I had already accepted that he belonged to Frances, not me, but discovering that he had fallen in love with another woman, one who was neither his wife nor me, was unbearable.
[1843] For a moment, I did consider punishing him, but the thought quickly faded. I wanted to make him as miserable as he had made me. So, I concocted a vile lie and told it to – oh, my God, told it to Harriet Spear. The lie I fabricated soothed my heart: the sweet nothings Patrick had said to Nancy, words I had overheard more than once, were actually spoken by the late William Dunn, and Patrick was just the messenger. I know it sounds absurd, but when I told Harriet Spear, for a few seconds, I believed it was true.
[1844] Then, Harriet got to work. She spread the rumor throughout the village that Patrick was terrible and unforgivable, and Alice and Richard helped her fan the flames. I never understood why they did this, knowing that Harriet had become a malicious person, and everyone in the village knew it. Why did they join her in accusing Patrick? Oh, I know the answer now: it was all my fault. Alice and Richard knew that Harriet was not the culprit, that the rumor came from Patrick's maid, who was loyal to Patrick and had no reason to lie.
[1845] I soon realized that jealousy had driven me to do a terrible and despicable thing. I watched as Patrick suffered, and I tried desperately to help him, as well as Frances, but I didn't know what to do! Harriet had seen Nancy sneaking into the parsonage at night, and Richard Nixes had seen it too. If I admitted to lying, I would have to come up with another explanation for Nancy's late-night visits to Patrick. But it wouldn't be long before Harriet figured out the real reason on her own.
[1846] And shamefully, I'm a coward. Everyone likes Richard and Eadie, and once they believe they're right, they don't care about what others think. But I do. I always try to make a good impression, and if I admit to lying now, everyone in the village will hate me, and that's understandable. I'm not a strong person, Mr. Poirot. I did nothing and said nothing because I was afraid. Then, Nancy stepped forward, terrified by the lies and shocked that people would believe them. She revealed the truth: she and Patrick loved each other, but there was no physical relationship between them.
[1847] Nancy's efforts only made things worse for Patrick. People began to say that he not only deceived the parishioners, disregarded the teachings, but was also an adulterous lover. Frances couldn't bear it anymore and committed suicide. Patrick discovered her dead and realized that he couldn't continue living with such a huge sin on his conscience, especially since the source of the trouble was his love for Nancy. He felt he had betrayed Frances and chose to commit suicide as well.
[1848] The village doctor claimed that they died from accidents, but that's not true. They both committed suicide. In the eyes of zealots like Eadie Gransbury and people like Harriet Sippel who enjoy condemning others, suicide is also a grave sin. You know, Patrick and Frances left wills. I found them and handed them over to Ambrose Flavard, the doctor. I think he burned them, saying he wouldn't give anyone another chance to accuse Patrick and Frances. Flavard hated how the villagers blamed them.
[1849] Patrick's death broke my heart. From that day on, my heart died, Mr. Poirot. I wanted to die, but then I thought, since Patrick died, I need to live on, loving and thinking of him, as if that could somehow make up for what he was slandered as the devil by everyone in Gleeholm!
[1850] The
[1851] Richard came to see me before returning to Devonshire, his brother's house, and directly asked me. I wanted to tell him that none of the rumors were true, but I dared not, so I said nothing. I just sat there silently, as if my tongue had been cut off. Richard took my silence as an admission of guilt.
[1852] He left Greshamsbury, and I left soon after. At first, I went to see Sem, but I could not stay in Cambridge, where there were too many memories of Patrick, so I came to London. It was Sem's idea. He found a job here and got friends to introduce me to work as well. Sem loves me as much as I love Patrick, and I should be grateful to him. He proposed to me again, but I refused, treating him as a very good friend.
[1853] After moving to London, my life began again. But I could not enjoy it, constantly missing Patrick, and feeling unbearable pain when I thought of never seeing him again.
[1854] Last September, I received a letter from Richard Nigges. Fifteen years have passed, but I do not feel that the matter is settled, for I have never let go! My address in London, known only to one person in Greshamsbury, Dr. Ambrose Flavard. He gave it to Richard. I do not know why I told him my address, maybe hoping that someone would know where I am. I remember thinking that I could not just disappear without a trace. I had a feeling…
[1855] No, I do not want to say that. I do not want to admit that at that time I hoped that someday in the future, Richard would come to me, begging for my help to correct the past mistakes. I would rather call it a strong premonition, although I cannot put it into words. I knew that Greshamsbury village and I would always be connected, and I could not escape Greshamsbury. That is why I had to tell Dr. Flavard my address in London.
[1856] Richard said in the letter that he would come to see me, and I didn't refuse. The following week, he came, and as soon as he saw me, he asked if I was willing to help make up for the unforgivable mistake committed many years ago.
[1857] I told him that I didn't think it could be made up for, as Patrick was dead, and the facts couldn't be changed. Richard said, "Yes, Patrick and Frances are both dead, and we haven't tasted happiness since then, but what if we made the necessary sacrifices?”
[1858] I didn't understand and asked what he meant.
[1859] He said, "Since it was us who killed Patrick and Frances, I think it's only right that we use our own lives to repay, isn't it? Can't we enjoy the joys of life like others? Why? So many years have passed, and why hasn't time healed our wounds? Is it because we shouldn't be alive, while poor Patrick and Frances are buried in the ground?"
[1860] Richard's words stirred a strong resonance in me, and although many people might say he was wrong, I felt he was right. As he spoke, my heart was once again filled with a similar feeling of hope. It had been fifteen years, the first time. Although it couldn't bring Patrick back to life, I could ensure that I received a fair judgment for what I had done to him and paid the price for my actions.
[1861] "Do you want me to commit suicide?" he hinted, so I asked him directly.
[1862] "No, I won't commit suicide. What I'm thinking of is being sentenced to death – we voluntarily choose to be sentenced to death. Or rather, I want to do it, I don't want to force you."
[1863] "Guilty people are not just you and me." I reminded him.
[1864] "Yes, not just us." He agreed, and what he said next nearly made my heart stop. "Jenny, would you be surprised to know that Harriet Sipprell and Ada Gransbury also repented and agreed with my thoughts?"
[1865] I told him I didn't believe it. I think Harriet and Ada will never admit to doing something so cruel and unforgivable. Richard said he used to think so too. He went on to say, "I convinced them. Jane, I'm good at convincing people, everyone listens to me. I put in the work on Harriet and Ada. I didn't harshly condemn them, but I kept feeding them my deep regret and my desire to make up for the harm caused. It took a long time – from the last time we spoke to now – Harriet and Ada finally began to see things my way. You know, they're both very unfortunate women. Harriet lost her husband, and I called off my engagement with Ada."
[1866] I opened my mouth to express my skepticism, but Richard continued. He assured me that Harriet and Ada realized they were responsible for Patrick and Francis Ives' deaths and wanted to make amends for their mistakes. Don't forget, she always longed to reunite with her husband in heaven and couldn't bear to be separated from him after death."
[1867] I was speechless with surprise. I said I would never believe what he said. But Richard told me that if I talked to Harriet and Ada, I would believe it. He said I had to see them and see how much they had changed.
[1868] I couldn't imagine Harriet or Ada changing and was worried that if I were in the same room with them, I would have a murderous impulse.
[1869] Richard said, "Jenny, you must understand that I gave them a way to escape their suffering – they were undoubtedly suffering. No one can cause such great harm to others and have their own soul intact. For years, Harriet and Ada believed that the only way to escape was to believe that everything they did to Patrick was right, but over time, they gradually realized that this path I pointed out was better: true forgiveness from God. Jenny, guilty souls yearn for redemption. The more we deny this opportunity for redemption, the more painful we will be. With my persistent efforts, Harriet and Ada's self-loathing grew stronger, and they felt disgusted by the fact that they had maliciously harmed others and clung to the false accusation against Patrick Ives."
[1870] Listening to Richard's story, I began to believe that even the most stubborn person, even Harriet Sipper, could be convinced. He had a way of making you see things differently.
[1871] He asked me if I could bring Harriet and Ada the next time we met, and although I was shocked and scared, I agreed.
[1872] Although I believed Richard after he left, two days later, when I met Harriet Sipper and Ada Gransbury and saw that they had indeed changed as he described, I was still shocked. To be precise, they were still the same as before, but now they were pointing the finger at themselves. However, when they talked about "poor, kind Patrick" and "poor, innocent Francis," my hatred would reignite, and they didn't deserve to say such things.
[1873] We all agreed that we must correct our past mistakes. We were murderers, even though the law did not convict us, but the fact remained unchanged, and murder deserved the sacrifice of one's own life. Only by dying could we obtain God's forgiveness.
[1874] Richard said, "We are the judges, the jury, and the executioners. We will carry out each other's death sentences."
[1875] Ada admiringly looked at him and asked, "What should we do?"
[1876] "I have thought of a way," he said. "I will take care of the details."
[1877] Thus, without argument or complaint, we signed our own death warrants. I felt a great sense of relief. As long as my victims were not afraid to die, I was not afraid to kill. Although the word 'victim' was not appropriate, I could not think of a better one.
[1878] At this point, Harriet said, "Wait a minute, what about Nancy Dunn?"
[1879] Without waiting for her explanation, I understood. I silently thought, yes, Harriet Spear had not changed. For her, it was not enough to justify the death of four people; she wanted a fifth life.
[1880] Richard and Ada asked her what she meant.

[1882] "Oh, no," I said alertly. "Nancy would never give up her life, and... Patrick loves her!"

[1886] I prayed for Nancy. I never blamed her for Patrick's death, never, and never would.
[1887] "Well!" said Richard, looking unhappy, "I don't want to say this, but Nancy Duane should not have been involved with a married man, and she should not have done it in such a way, making a public scandal of her improper relationship with Patrick. [1890] I wanted to shout, to run out of the room, but I forced myself to sit there. I was certain that Harriet had invented the reason for Nancy's confession at the inn. [1892] Richard said we told Harriet and Ada that we would kill each other in accordance with our agreement, and then pin the blame on Nancy Duane. Since Nancy lived in London, the action would have to take place in London. Richard suggested finding a hotel and said he would pay for everything.
[1893] Once we arrived at the hotel, things would be simple. Ada would kill Harriet, Richard would kill Ada, and I would kill Richard. Each murderer would place a cufflink engraved with the initial of Patrick Yew's first name in the mouth of the victim and arrange the scene to look exactly the same as the previous ones, so that the police would naturally assume that these... deaths, were the work of the same killer. I wanted to say murder, but it wasn't, it was an execution. You see, what we were thinking was that after the executions, there would be a ceremony, wouldn't there? We thought the prison staff would do the same thing to each death row inmate.
[1894] Because two of the victims, Ada and Harriet, had written their home addresses in Gravelhinge when they checked into the hotel, we knew that the police would soon go to the village to investigate and then quickly suspect Nancy. Yes, it should be three keys, the plan also included Richard's key. Ada was to lock the door with Ada's key after killing Harriet and bring the key back to her room. Richard did the same: after killing Ada, he locked the door and took both Harriet and Ada's keys with him. Finally, I killed Richard, locked his door, took all the keys, and then met Semple outside the Blakes Hotel and gave him all the keys. Semple would find an opportunity to sneak into Nancy Duane's house or seize the opportunity on the street to quietly slip the keys into her coat pocket to prove her guilt.
[1895] I didn't care much about the cufflinks, as Patrick Yew never wore clothes with letter cufflinks. As far as I know, he didn't have a pair. Richard Nigg decided to order the cufflinks specifically to mislead the police in the right direction. The bloodstains in the fourth room and my hat were also part of our plan, intended to make you believe that I died in that room and that Nancy Duane killed us four for her lover's revenge. Richard let Semple find blood, and if you want to know, I can tell you that it was the blood of a stray cat. The note left at the front desk was also left by Semple. "May they rest in peace," and the numbers of the three rooms. Eight o'clock later, while no one was paying attention, he placed the note at the front desk. As for my task, it was to stay alive until I confirmed that Nancy Duane was hanged for killing three people. If the police thought I was dead too, that would be four.
[1896] How should I proceed? As the fourth person Nancy needs to kill, the fourth person who must be responsible for Patrick's death, I need to let the police know that I'm scared, scared that someone wants my life. Your other suspicions are also correct; I did hear from the waiter that a detective from Europe comes every Thursday at 7:30 p.m., sometimes having dinner with an old friend from Scotland Yard. [1897] However, Mr. Poirot, one of your conclusions is incorrect. Because by then, I had already destroyed everything. When I said that sentence, I was only thinking about the plan I had with Richard, which was for me to survive. So, you see, they could very well have been alive when I said that.
[1898] I should make it clear, we have two plans: one that both Harriet and Ada agree on; and another one that only Richard and I know about, and the two plans are completely different. I must be the last one to die because implementing this plan requires acting, and I am very good at acting. To naturally meet you in the café, Mr. Poirot... Harriet can't pull that off. Richard and Ada can't either. So you see, I must live to the end.
[1899] But the plans that Harriet and Ada knew were not Richard's true plans. [1927] Jane nodded.
[1928] I finally understood why the scene was as it was: the three bodies were in identical positions, between the small coffee table and the chairs, upright with feet facing the door. As Mr. Poirot said, it was impossible for Harriet Spear, Ada Gransbury, and Richard Nixes to all end up in that position.
[1929] The similarities between the three murder scenes were suspicious, and now I understood why: the conspirators wanted the police to believe there was only one killer. On the same night, at the same hotel, three bodies, with a cufflink in each mouth. Any competent detective could have deduced this was the work of one person, and an obsessive one at that. But they knew there were more than one killer, so they were afraid, afraid of the truth being revealed, so they went to great lengths to make the three murder scenes look very similar, even overdoing it.
[1930] The bodies lying neatly on the ground, upright and identical, also aligned with their intentions: what happened at Browns Hotel wasn't a murder, but an execution. After each execution, there were subsequent procedures, like a religious ritual. I thought it was better to arrange the bodies properly than to leave them lying around, not looking like the work of an ordinary killer or a garden killer.
[1931] Suddenly, a younger Jane Hobbs appeared in my mind, tidying up the beds in the rooms at Jesus College, Cambridge, one by one. She would arrange each bed in the same pattern...
[1932] Beds, death beds...
[1933] Pattern, disrupted pattern...
[1934] "Richard Naylegh committed suicide." I blurted out involuntarily, "It was suicide. That's why he hid the key behind the tiles in the fireplace, pretending that the killer took the key and then escaped through the window. If the key was found, we would have been suspicious - and we were indeed puzzled - why the killer locked the door from the inside and hid the key in the room, yet escaped through the window. But we would still believe there was only one killer. Naylegh only cared about that. But if the window was closed and the key was found, we would only conclude that Richard Naylegh committed suicide. He would not take the risk of letting us draw that conclusion, right? Once we came to that conclusion, the plot to frame Nancy Duane for the murders of three people would fail, and we would be more likely to believe that Naylegh first killed Harriet Spear and Ada Gransbury and then committed suicide."

[2131] "Only by finding the answers to all the questions can you be sure you're right," Poirot didn't listen to me and continued, "If some answers can't be found, you'll find that the details that seemed to fit together actually don't."
[2132] "Right now, if I think of my brain as a crossword puzzle, there's not a single letter filled in."
[2133] "Not for long, my friend. Not for long. Poirot will conduct another investigation in the restaurant of the Brocks Hotel!"
[2134] Chapter XXII
[2135] The Murder of the Alphabet Cufflinks
[2136] At 4:15 pm the next day, Poirot and I stood at the far end of the Blore House Hotel restaurant, waiting for all the staff to take their seats at the tables. I must say, I could never understand why he called so many people here, as if we were putting on a play in a theater. After solving a case - and I was lucky, even without Poirot's help, I had solved several cases - I would only submit a report to my boss and arrest the culprit.

[2209] Chapter XXIII
[2210] The True Adah Gransbury

[2253] Chapter 24
[2254] The Blue Pot and Bowl
[2255] There were a few screams of terror in the dining room, and I almost screamed myself. It was too strange! Thanks to my work at Scotland Yard, I've seen many corpses, but none were as terrifying as the sight of a dead woman sitting upright, as if she were enjoying tea with her friends.

[2260] This was a good question. Each point Poirot made was on target and something I should have thought of. Regardless, I hadn't noticed how many inconsistencies there were in Miss Hobbs' statement. How could I have overlooked so many obvious gaps?
[2261] Poirot said, "The person pretending to be Richard Nigges was only trying to make Rafael Borker see him at 7:15 and 7:30 to see Thomas Brine. He didn't care about the bills! [2483] Epilogue

The bloodstains dragged towards the door were a deception, intended to make us believe that the body was moved elsewhere. This detail was done very convincingly, so it was very important as evidence at the crime scene.
[2502] "But for Hercule Poirot, it was just a small detail that allowed him to verify his suspicions. Jeanie Hobbs was not killed in that room, and no one was killed. But I can think of containers that can easily be brought into the room and directly put the body in and take it away, without dragging the body to the door. Caderpillar, this logic is very simple.
